Pro Private Markets
VC Focus: Venture-Backed Companies Prepare to Exit Through Potential Fall IPO Window

By Russ Garland with reports from Yuliya Chernova, Deborah Gage, Tomio Geron, Patience Haggin and Cat Zakrzewski 
1,384 words
29 September 2016
14:12
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Venture capitalists are hoping a little action this fall will improve what has been a dismal year for initial public offerings in the U.S.

The first half of 2016 saw the fewest IPOs by venture-backed companies since 2009. As of Sept. 11, there were 22 IPOs, all but four of them by health-care companies. Many of the health-care offerings needed an assist from their venture backers.

See also

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/health-care-ipos-get-boost-from-venture-capital-investors-1475154543"

* "http://www.wsj.com/pro/venturecapital/graphics/ipo-index/"

Uncertain public markets and falling valuations of venture-backed companies have discouraged technology companies from taking the plunge this year, but the outlook improved during the summer. Newly public companies have generally performed well. The WSJ Pro Venture-Backed Post-IPO Index, which measures company performance for two years after an IPO, had risen for the half from a low of 672.94 in February to 996.64 as of Sept. 12.

There are plenty of candidates if the market looks favorable. As of mid-September, there were 92 U.S.-based companies on The Wall Street Journal's Billion Dollar Startup Club, a compilation of venture-backed companies valued at $1 billion or more.

Companies are starting to queue up. Focus Financial Partners, for instance, filed confidentially for its IPO and might seek to list by the end of the year, The Journal previously reported. Backed by Centerbridge Partners, Polaris Partners and Summit Partners, the company helps wealth managers set up and expand their businesses.

There were 26 companies in public registration for an IPO as of Sept. 11, according to VentureSource, a data provider owned by Private Equity Analyst publisher Dow Jones & Co., and there are surely many more that have filed confidentially under the federal Jumpstart Our Business Startups Act. This enables them to have their prospective IPO filing reviewed by the Securities and Exchange Commission and to test the waters with qualified potential investors. Once they file publicly, such companies should be in a position to launch an IPO within a few weeks.

Here is a rundown of some companies that are strong prospects to stage an IPO in the next six months.

MapR Technologies Inc.

Big-data software companies are emerging as potential IPO candidates, despite falling valuations in this area. MapR Technologies Inc. founder John Schroeder, for instance, said his company is chasing profitability and could be ready for an IPO in early 2017.

MapR, of San Jose, Calif., was supposed to be cash-flow positive by the end of 2015, according to Private Equity Analyst records, but that goal was delayed. In September, Mr. Schroeder stepped down as chief executive in favor of Matt Mills, a former sales executive from Oracle Corp. MapR combines the advantages of free open-source software like Hadoop, which was developed by and for computer scientists and is hard to use, with proprietary software that makes it easier.

The company is now valued at about $500 million, according to data provider PitchBook Data Inc., more than its competitor Hortonworks Inc., which was valued at more than $1 billion when it went public in late 2014.

Another competitor, Cloudera Inc., raised $670 million from Intel Capital and other investors and was last valued in March 2014 at $4.1 billion. Although that valuation likely has fallen, Cloudera's share price has been picking up again this year, according to The Journal's Startup Stock Tracker, raising hopes that it could go public despite its high valuation.

TechStyle Fashion Group

TechStyle Fashion Group, the company behind fashion brands such as JustFab and ShoeDazzle, has been getting more serious. It renamed itself from the more carefree Just Fabulous Inc. It also brought in a chief financial officer with IPO experience this summer. Todd Tappin previously was CFO at advertising technology company Rubicon Project Inc., while the company completed an IPO in 2014.

TechStyle's business model uses monthly memberships to create more predictable revenue streams. On one of its Web properties, JustFab.com, a women's fashion site, members are charged $39.95 each month, which can be used as credit to make purchases or to save. Another unusual aspect of TechStyle is that it uses a personalized quiz that shoppers take when they sign up. This helps the company personalize offerings, which it says increases the likelihood of purchases.

TechStyle says it has more than 4 million members and has shipped about 50 million products. The company has been expanding its product lines to include children's and men's fashions. It also has physical locations at stores called Fabletics. The company opened its 15th Fabletics store in September.

The El Segundo, Calif.-based company was valued at $1 billion in a 2014 funding round. It has raised $279 million to date. Among its investors are Matrix Partners and Technology Crossover Ventures.

AppNexus Inc.

The ad-tech industry may be pinning its hopes on one long-awaited IPO. AppNexus Inc., a platform that enables buyers and sellers to auction off advertising space according to purchasing algorithms, may be waiting in the wings. Industry publication AdExchanger reported the company is working to file for a public offering. The New York startup, valued at $1.8 billion, has raised more than $250 million from investors including First Round Capital, Khosla Ventures and British advertising firm WPP PLC. It claims profitability and a series of acquisitions.

Last year, the New York company made very public moves to eliminate ad fraud from its supply pool. The company claims the crackdown has boosted its ad prices, allowing it to power the same amount of ad spending despite a dip in volume.

Other ad-tech companies such as Rocket Fuel Inc. and Criteo SA have struggled after going public. As ad blockers have grown in popularity in recent years, public and private investors alike have grown skeptical of the category. But the opportunity for ad-tech public offerings may have reopened in September, when ad-buying platform Trade Desk Inc. listed on the Nasdaq Stock Market and saw its stock jump by more than 60% on its first day of trading to a closing price of $30.10 per share, from an initial price of $18 per share.

Okta Inc.

Okta Inc., a member of the Billion Dollar Startup Club, has signaled it is considering an IPO through a job listing for an assistant controller on its website. In the description for the position, the San Francisco startup called for a candidate who can help the company maintain a staff that can "support our pre- and post-IPO financial reporting environment." Okta declined to comment.

Okta last announced financing a year ago, when it raised an additional $75 million, valuing the company at $1.2 billion. Its investors include Andreessen Horowitz, Greylock Partners and Sequoia Capital. The company produces an identity management tool that enables employees to access enterprise applications on any device.

Dropbox Inc.

Dropbox Inc., a prominent private company that has raised more than $1 billion in funding, was valued at $10 billion in 2014. But since then, investors including BlackRock Inc. have cut their estimates of the company's valuation.

That high $10 billion valuation may pose a problem to Dropbox if it seeks an IPO because the company and its investors may not want to go public until its valuation is above or at least close to its last private valuation. Competitor Box Inc., meanwhile, went public in early 2015 and has a market capitalization of $1.88 billion. It reported a loss of $38.1 million on $95.7 million in revenue for its most recent quarter. Box focuses on enterprise customers. Dropbox has more than 500 million users but most don't pay. That provides a pool of potential sales leads, but Wall Street will want to see how many of them are converting to paid customers.

The San Francisco company has been focusing on business customers and says it has 150,000 businesses that pay for its service. Dropbox in June announced a partnership with Hewlett Packard Enterprise Co. aimed at gaining more such corporate customers.

Pro Private Markets
The Roundup

5,094 words
29 September 2016
14:04
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.S. & CANADA

BUYOUTS

Align Capital Partners, Cleveland

Align Capital Partners, formed by longtime Riverside Co. executives, closed its first pool of capital for lower midmarket deals. The vehicle, which consists of Align Capital Partners Fund I LP and Align Capital Partners Fund I-A LP, held a first and final closing at its $325 million hard cap, beating its $250 million target. The new firm is led by managing partners Steve Dyke, Chris Jones and Rob Langley, former Riverside deal makers who spun out from the parent firm in March. Align is targeting North American businesses with less than $10 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization within the specialty manufacturing, distribution and business-services sectors, with a focus on business-to-business companies. The founders specialize in companies that haven't been under institutional ownership before, said Mr. Jones. Align, which plans to write equity checks of $15 million to $30 million, aims to make 10 to 12 investments from the fund.

Apollo Global Management LLC, New York

Apollo Global Management LLC raised at least $3.15 billion for its second natural resources fund, Apollo Natural Resources Partners II LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund held a first close on about $1.33 billion in September, Private Equity Analyst previously reported, citing a regulatory filing and a person familiar with the situation. Its predecessor closed on more than $1.3 billion in 2012.

Folium Capital, Boston

Folium Capital, a new natural resources-focused firm co-founded by Andrew Wiltshire, who formerly headed alternative assets at Harvard Management Co., secured its first investor commitments. The firm is seeking about $500 million between its debut agriculture and timber funds, said a person with knowledge of the matter. Folium raised at least $50 million for Folium Agriculture Fund I LP and $21.3 million for a parallel pool, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Folium disclosed it raised the same amounts for Folium Timber Fund I LP and another companion vehicle.

Gamut Capital Management, New York

Gamut Capital Management, a firm launched last year by former Apollo Global Management LLC executives Jordan Zaken and Stan Parker, is expected to close Gamut Investment Fund I LP at its $1 billion hard cap this fall, people familiar with the matter said. Gamut initially set a $750 million goal for the fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Gamut plans to take a flexible approach that resembles Apollo's, but scaled to smaller investments. It will buy both debt and equity and will make deals in a wide range of industries, generally investing $40 million to $125 million at a time, according to a regulatory filing. Like Apollo, it will tailor its strategy to the market cycle. When liquidity is readily available, the firm will make buyouts and corporate carve-out investments, but when the market turns and liquidity dries up, it will make distress-for-control deals, according to the filing. The Louisiana State Employees' Retirement System committed $50 million to the fund, according to a pension document.

Kayne Anderson Capital Advisors, Los Angeles

Kayne Anderson Capital Advisors held a final closing for Kayne Private Energy Income Fund LP with $1.55 billion in total capital commitments. The vehicle, which is part of a unit that oversees energy-focused private-equity investments, will acquire producing oil and gas assets in North America. Kayne Anderson managed about $22.5 billion in assets as of June 30 for institutional investors, family offices and high-net-worth and retail clients.

Kohlberg & Co., Mount Kisco, N.Y.

Kohlberg & Co.'s eighth fund has raised at least $1.7 billion so far, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The sum for the firm's eighth vehicle includes money committed to Kohlberg Investors VIII LP and related vehicles, according to the filings. Kohlberg's seventh fund held a final closing on $1.6 billion in 2013. Kohlberg invests $50 million to $300 million of equity in North American companies with enterprise values of $100 million to $1 billion. The firm targets companies within the industrial manufacturing, consumer products, business services, financial services and health-care services sectors.

Madison Dearborn Partners, Chicago

Madison Dearborn Partners held a final closing on $4.4 billion for its seventh fund, Madison Dearborn Capital Partners VII LP. The firm is purchasing Intermedia.net Inc., a cloud-based business applications provider, from Oak Hill Capital Partners through the fund, which was seeking $3.75 billion. Madison Dearborn closed on $4.1 billion for its sixth pool in 2010, below both an initial target of $10 billion set in late 2007 and a revised $7.5 billion target set in 2008, The Wall Street Journal previously reported. Sectors the firm invests in include telecommunications, media and technologies services; financial and transaction services; business and government services; health care; basic industries; and the consumer realm.

Summit Partners, Boston

Summit Partners collected at least $2.35 billion for its ninth growth-equity fund, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The amount raised so far for Summit Partners Growth Equity Fund IX puts the vehicle nearly three quarters of the way toward a $3.18 billion offering amount indicated in the filings. Summit announced a final closing of its prior growth-equity fund at $2.7 billion in 2012. Investors in the latest fund include the Minnesota State Board of Investment, Pennsylvania Public Schools Employees' Retirement System and Texas County and District Retirement System. Summit itself contributed about $141 million in general partner commitments to the fund, according to the filings. The firm invests $10 million to $500 million at a time in companies. In addition to its growth-equity funds, Summit also manages venture-capital and private-debt funds.

Swander Pace Capital, San Francisco

Midmarket consumer investor Swander Pace Capital closed its sixth fund, SPC Partners VI LP, at $510 million, exceeding the vehicle's initial $400 million goal. The firm's previous fund closed at $350 million in 2013. Swander Pace targets investments in businesses with up to $400 million in annual revenue in consumer subcategories such as health and wellness, food and beverage, and home products.

Thoma Bravo, Chicago

Thoma Bravo, a firm focused on the software and technology-enabled services sectors, wrapped up fundraising for its latest flagship vehicle with $7.6 billion. Thoma Bravo Fund XII LP reached its hard cap and is larger than the firm's previous flagship vehicle, Thoma Bravo Fund XI LP, which closed in 2014 at $3.65 billion. Fund XII is expected to make investments of $200 million to $1 billion, according to an investor memorandum. Investors in the fund include the New York State Common Retirement Fund, New York State Teachers' Retirement System and Los Angeles City Employees' Retirement System. Orlando Bravo, a managing partner at Thoma Bravo, said the latest fund allows the firm to target midmarket deals as well as write larger checks, as it did in the case of its $3 billion take-private of Qlik Technologies Inc., which closed in August. The firm also in September closed a take-private deal for health-care information-technology security company Imprivata Inc. The deal was valued at $544 million when it was announced in July.

True Wind Capital Management, San Francisco

A young technology-focused firm launched by Adam Clammer and Jaime Greene, former partners at KKR & Co., is closing in on a $400 million target for its debut fund, said people familiar with the matter. True Wind Capital Management sealed $381.1 million for its True Wind Capital LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission, and continues to raise money, said a person with knowledge of the firm. The firm hasn't yet set a limit on the fund's size, the person said, leaving the door open for it to gather more money than it is targeting. True Wind will focus on enterprise technology, according to a separate SEC filing.

Wasserstein & Co., New York

Wasserstein Partners, the private-equity arm of investment firm Wasserstein & Co., completed fundraising for Wasserstein Partners IV LP, which closed with $790 million, said a person familiar with the matter. The sum exceeds a $750 million goal disclosed in a Securities and Exchange Commission filing last year. The firm's third fund closed with $403 million in 2014. Wasserstein Partners invests in businesses focused on media and communications, consumer products and industrial processes related to water management. The firm targets companies with $10 million to $50 million in annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization and aims to make equity investments of $50 million to $200 million. Through the new fund, Wasserstein Partners acquired Northstar Travel Media LLC, a provider of business-to-business information for the travel industry, from Wicks Group of Cos. in June.

DISTRESSED/TURNAROUND

Farallon Capital Management, San Francisco

Farallon Capital Management collected $400 million for Farallon Real Estate Partners II LP, a new fund targeting distressed real-estate deals in the U.S. Farallon's previous fund closed at $375 million in 2014. Sixteen of the 17 limited partners in the first fund returned for the second, said Richard Fried, a managing member at the firm. The Farallon real-estate team aims to make investments of $10 million to $50 million in undervalued properties including office, industrial, retail and apartment properties, and then turn them around.

Stellex Capital Management, New York

Stellex Capital Management, a group founded by executives who previously ran Carlyle Group LP's distressed team, gathered at least $586 million for Stellex Capital Partners LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund is seeking $750 million, according to the filing. Investors in the fund include the New York State Common Retirement Fund, Teacher Retirement System of Texas and California Public Employees' Retirement System. Stellex typically invests $25 million to $100 million and more through co-investments in North American and European companies with enterprise values of $50 million to $500 million within various industrial and services sectors.

MEZZANINE & OTHER DEBT

Peninsula Capital Partners, Detroit

Peninsula Capital Partners held a final closing on $402 million for Peninsula Fund VI LP to invest in lower midmarket companies. The fund will invest $5 million to $50 million of subordinated debt and equity at a time and has closed on five deals. The firm invests in the U.S. and Canada and is a sector generalist, though about 60% of its deals are in manufacturing, industrial services and distribution, said Scott Reilly, Peninsula's president and chief investment officer. Its predecessor fund closed on $389 million in 2010.

SECONDARY

Blackstone Group LP, New York

Blackstone Group LP collected about $6.55 billion so far for its latest secondary pool, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The capital raised for Strategic Partners Fund VII LP and a parallel offshore vehicle roughly matches the $6.5 billion hard cap cited in a February document from the Pennsylvania Public School Employees' Retirement System. A person with knowledge of the fundraising process said the vehicle hasn't yet held a final closing. Penn PSERS and Tennessee Consolidated Retirement System said they would commit to the fund. Fund VII will buy mature fund portfolios that are generally fully invested or nearly so, according to a letter from Portfolio Advisors LLC, an adviser to Penn PSERS. It will allocate about 75% of its capital to buyouts, 10% to venture capital, 10% to mezzanine funds and 5% to other types of funds, according to the letter. Blackstone's Strategic Partners unit closed its previous fund at $4.4 billion in 2014. The firm also reached the $1.3 billion hard cap for its sixth real estate-focused fund, Strategic Partners Real Estate Fund VI LP. The fund will buy real-estate and co-investment stakes from investors looking for liquidity.

Goldman Sachs Asset Management, New York

Goldman Sachs Asset Management collected more than $4 billion for a fund to purchase secondhand stakes in private-equity funds, according to a person familiar with the matter. The fund management arm of Goldman Sachs Group Inc. may raise as much as $6 billion for the vehicle, the person said. Goldman Sachs Asset Management hasn't yet completed any deals from the new vehicle but will begin investing in the market for secondhand fund stakes in the fall, the person said. The arm's previous secondary fund closed in 2013 on $5.8 billion.

Kline Hill Partners, Greenwich, Conn.

Kline Hill Partners is in the process of wrapping up its debut secondary fund, which is expected to hit or exceed its $180 million cap, said people with knowledge of the vehicle. The firm has closed on a total of about $128.5 million so far for Kline Hill Partners Fund LP and Kline Hill Partners Offshore Fund LP, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings and a person with knowledge of the offering. The fund received limited partner interest in excess of $180 million, the people said. Kline Hill targets secondary investments in smaller transactions that typically would fall under the radar of most larger secondary buyers, one person said.

VENTURE CAPITAL

AutoTech Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

AutoTech Ventures raised at least $44.8 million for AutoTech Fund I LP, which is seeking $150 million, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm, which invests $500,000 to $5 million in ground transportation startups, so far has announced only one investment in Lyft Inc., but has made two more investments it has yet to announce, said Managing Director Quin Garcia. The firm's limited partners include a number of auto parts makers and component suppliers, including Denso Corp., Mahle GmbH and Murata Manufacturing Co.

Brooklyn Bridge Ventures, Brooklyn, N.Y.

Charlie O'Donnell, the sole general partner of Brooklyn Bridge Ventures, wrapped up his second fund at $15.1 million. Mr. O'Donnell said the fund will allow him to write checks of about $350,000 per deal. He is about to make his sixth deal out of the fund. Mr. O'Donnell's first fund closed in 2014 with $8.3 million. Limited partners in the latest vehicle include Two Sigma Ventures, as well as individuals such as Jeff Citron, Vonage chairman and managing partner at KEC Ventures, and a university endowment, which Mr. O'Donnell declined to name. The fund consists of two vehicles, Brooklyn Bridge Ventures II LP and Brooklyn Bridge Ventures II-A LP, with bigger LPs in one fund and individual investors in the other.

Bryant Stibel, Malibu, Calif.

Retired NBA star Kobe Bryant and longtime entrepreneur and investor Jeff Stibel unveiled Bryant Stibel, a $100 million venture-capital fund for investing in technology, media and data companies. The two men are contributing the $100 million which they expect to invest over the next few years and aren't seeking outside investors yet. Messrs. Bryant and Stibel have been invested in 15 companies since 2013, but only after Mr. Bryant's retirement from basketball have they decided to formalize their relationship and fund. Current investments include sports media website Players Tribune Inc., videogame designer Scopely Inc., legal-services company LegalZoom.com Inc., telemarketing-software firm RingDNA and home-juicing company Juicero Inc.

Drive Capital, Columbus, Ohio

Drive Capital closed its second fund, Drive Capital Fund II LP, at $300 million. The firm was founded by former Sequoia Capital partners based in Menlo Park, Calif., who decided the Midwest offered better opportunities than Silicon Valley for building companies. Drive Capital is targeting the broader Midwest, including Ann Arbor, Mich., Pittsburgh and several other cities based on the quality of the region's universities and potential corporate customers and the idea that it is now easier to start a company anywhere, the firm said. The firm, which began fundraising for its debut fund in 2013, ultimately closed the vehicle with $250 million.

First Round Capital, Philadelphia

Seed-stage investor First Round Capital raised about $175 million for First Round Capital VI LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm's investments include food service Blue Apron Inc., hotel-booking app Hotel Tonight Inc. and ride-hailing app Uber Technologies Inc. First Round raised $175 million for its latest fund's predecessor in 2014.

Founders Circle Capital, San Francisco

Founders Circle Capital, a firm that buys secondary stakes in later-stage startups such as Pinterest Inc., raised $208 million for its second fund. The firm previously raised $195 million across two funds in 2014, and had invested from that fund in 24 companies. Founders Circle buys primarily common stock from individual founders and employees in these companies. Occasionally it will buy from early angel investors. The firm targets late-stage companies with at least $40 million in revenue, 40% annual revenue growth and 40% gross margins.

Hyde Park Venture Partners, Chicago

Hyde Park Venture Partners closed its second fund at $65 million, exceeding a $60 million goal. Founded in 2011, the firm backs software startups in the Midwest raising their first or second rounds of institutional capital. It announced the final closing of its first $25 million pool in 2013.

Lavrock Ventures, McLean, Va.

Lavrock Ventures, an early-stage investor within the cybersecurity and enterprise software sectors, is seeking $50 million for Lavrock Ventures Fund LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm didn't record any commitments for the fund at the time of the filing. Lavrock invests $1 million to $3 million, usually as a lead investor in rounds of $3 million to $10 million.

Rev1 Ventures, Columbus, Ohio

Rev1 Ventures raised a $22 million seed fund called Rev1 Fund I LLC to invest in Columbus-area startups across a range of technologies: software, hardware, life sciences, alternative energy and advanced materials. Rev1, in addition to investing, guides entrepreneurs on business strategy and connects them to local business services and companies. Some of the startups the company works with are formed around intellectual property from local research institutions, particularly in the area of data analytics, said Chief Executive Tom Walker. Backers of the fund include Nationwide Mutual Insurance Co., Ohio State University and Cardinal Health Inc. Ohio Third Frontier Pre-Seed/Seed-Plus Fund Capitalization Program, an initiative managed by the Ohio Development Services Agency that gives capital to early-stage Ohio technology companies, provided an $8 million loan to help Rev1 grow more quickly, Mr. Walker said.

Sapphire Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

Sapphire Ventures, the independent venture-capital firm that spun out of SAP SE, closed on $1 billion in new capital to invest in expansion-stage technology startups. The commitments bring the firm's total assets under management to more than $2.4 billion. Sapphire said its portfolio has seen 38 exits, including 15 initial public offerings and 23 mergers and acquisitions. Four of its portfolio companies went public last year: Apigee Corp., Box Inc., Fitbit Inc. and Square Inc. Sapphire also invests as a limited partner in early-stage venture funds in the U.S., Europe and Israel. Sapphire Managing Director Nino Marakovic said the firm remains focused on investing with firms that back companies at the Series A round.

Sofinnova Ventures, Menlo Park, Calif.

Biotechnology investor Sofinnova Ventures rounded up at least $595.5 million for a fund with a $650 million offering amount, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund, Sofinnova Venture Partners X LP, follows a $500 million fund closed in 2014. Sofinnova invests primarily in later-stage drug makers and targets sectors such as oncology, dermatology and respiratory diseases. Like many other biotech investors, the firm has seen several companies go public in recent years, including Audentes Therapeutics Inc., a developer of gene therapies for rare diseases that had its Nasdaq Stock Market debut in July, and Spark Therapeutics Inc., a company that has developed a potential gene therapy for a rare blinding disease. Spark went public last year.

Solas BioVentures, Chattanooga, Tenn.

Solas BioVentures, an early-stage medical investor, plans to seek up to $40 million to $100 million for a second fund in 2017, said co-founder David Adair. The firm held a final closing for its debut fund in April. Solas BioVentures Fund I LP raised $15 million to $25 million, Dr. Adair said. Solas has funded 12 companies and recently sold one of them. Details on the sale haven't been revealed yet because of the terms of the agreement, Dr. Adair said.

True Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

True Ventures, which has backed startups such as Fitbit Inc., Automattic Inc. and MakerBot Industries LLC, raised $310 million for True Ventures V LP. The firm, founded in 2005, raised $290 million for its prior fund in 2014. True is typically the first institutional investor in a startup and seeks to invest 70% to 100% of a round with $1 million to $3 million. The firm seeks larger positions than some smaller seed firms. True can also invest earlier than some larger traditional venture firms and then follow on in subsequent rounds, maintaining a large position. The firm has moved aggressively into new categories, hoping to plant its flag before other investors. Recently it has been active in startups combining biology and health care, such as genetics startup Deep Genomics, synthetic biology startup Zymergen Inc., mental health startup Ginger.io Inc. and personalized health firm Vital Labs. True has also been active in neuroscience, where it has invested in four startups.

Westly Group, Menlo Park, Calif.

Westly Group is seeking $150 million for its third fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm didn't disclose any commitments for Westly Capital Partners Fund III LP in the filing. Westly invests in clean-technology companies primarily in North America. The firm counts among its investments Building Robotics Inc., which makes a software platform called Comfy for managing building heating and cooling; and cybersecurity company Sentinel One Inc.

EUROPE

BUYOUTS

Ardian, Paris

Ardian closed its latest European midcap buyout fund, Ardian LBO Fund VI, on its  ¬4 billion ($4.46 billion) hard cap and gathered an additional  ¬500 million for co-investments. The amounts raised for the fund and for co-investments are both higher than the French firm's  ¬2.4 billion previous fund, which closed in 2013, and its accompanying  ¬400 million co-investment pool. More than two-thirds of investors in Ardian's LBO Fund V committed to the firm's latest fund. It also attracted 20 new investors to its latest vehicle, including institutional investors and sovereign-wealth funds, said Philippe Poletti, who heads the firm's midcap buyout practice. Ardian's latest buyout fund will invest in companies with enterprise values of  ¬200 million to  ¬600 million in sectors such as health care, chemicals, aeronautics and defense, insurance and food ingredients, Mr. Poletti said.

Blue Water Energy, London

Blue Water Energy, a firm that invests solely in the energy sector, recently held a $885 million first close of its latest fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The firm, which aims to raise $1 billion in total, plans to close the fund by the end of the year, the person said. Blue Water's $861 million debut vehicle, raised in 2013, is now about 80% invested with room for one or two more investments, the person said. The firm seeks to invest $150 per company and follows a buy-and-build strategy of providing initial capital followed by add-on acquisitions and growth capital.

CBPE Capital, London

CBPE Capital held a 459 million ($613 million) final close on its latest buyout vehicle, CBPE Capital Fund IX. The fund, which hit its hard cap, received commitments from 20 investors. Fund IX will make about 14 investments in U.K.-based companies with enterprise values of 25 million to 150 million. CBPE invests within the consumer, leisure and retail; health-care; industrials, business and financial services; and technology sectors. The firm's predecessor closed on 405 million in 2010.

EQT, Stockholm

EQT is seeking to raise  ¬1.4 billion ($1.57 billion) for midmarket deals in Europe. The firm hit a first close for its midmarket Europe fund at  ¬1.1 billion in July, according to a person familiar with the matter. It aims to close the vehicle, which has a  ¬1.6 billion upper limit, in the fall, the person said. The fund will invest up to  ¬100 million of equity in Northern European companies. EQT launched its debut midmarket fund with  ¬1.1 billion in 2013. That fund made investments in Northern Europe, China and Southeast Asia. EQT's current midmarket fund won't have a specific focus on Asia, the person said.

Livingbridge, London

Livingbridge raised 660 million ($876.4 million) for its sixth midmarket fund, Livingbridge 6. The vehicle is almost double the size of the firm's fifth vehicle, which collected 360 million in 2012. Wol Kolade, the firm's managing partner, said the firm began fundraising for the pool in May and saw a significant share of returning investors, with a "good 80%" of investors in previous funds "reupping" their commitments, combined with new interest from "a number of large, first-time U.S. investors [and] pension funds." Mr. Kolade said appetite from foreign investors accounted for 90% of the fund's investor base. WORLD NEWS
Singapore warned over financial crime record; 1MDB fallout

By Jeevan Vasagar in Singapore 
408 words
28 September 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
05
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Singapore has been urged to do more to combat money laundering in a report by a global watchdog that highlights the reputational risk posed by the 1MDB scandal in Malaysia.

The Financial Action Task Force said Singapore was effective in tackling smaller-scale financial crime involving "money mules" who courier cash for criminals but must strengthen the capability of law enforcement agencies to identify and investigate bigger, cross-border cases.

"Singapore should more aggressively target the more complex cases expected of a sophisticated financial centre," the intergovernmental body said.

The report, based on a review of Singapore 's measures against money laundering conducted late last year, finds that few foreign cases were prosecuted and low amounts of criminal proceeds were confiscated.

Singapore is conducting a wide-ranging inquiry into illicit fund flows linked to the 1MDB state investment fund of Malaysia. The city state's regulator admitted in July that the scandal had dented Singapore's reputation as a trusted financial centre, and pledged more "intrusive" inspections of banks .

Authorities have found lapses at a number of banks in Singapore that acted as conduits for flows of funds from 1MDB.

Singapore authorities said in response to yesterday's report that they intended to develop more sophisticated data analytics as part of a broader push to strengthen policing of financial crime.

The task force, based in Paris, also warned that the majority of Singapore's dealers in precious stones and metals are not covered by anti-money laundering supervision. "In contrast with the financial sector, the financial penalty structure . . . is quite diverse and enforcement of the sanctioning regime for non-compliance . . . is at an early stage," the report said.

The report says Singapore's transparency measures are insufficient to ensure information on beneficial owners - the people who ultimately own and control companies - is available "in a timely manner". Singapore should be more alert to the terrorist financing risks "associated with its geographical location and its position as a financial hub", it suggests.

Since the review, six Bangladeshi men have been jailed in Singapore on terrorist financing charges, on suspicion of planning attacks in their home country.

Malaysia's government has been rocked by allegations that about $4bn has been misappropriated from state companies, following investigations by regulators into 1MDB. Flows of money linked to the fund are under investigation in at least six countries.

Pro Private Markets
Malaysian Funds Shifting Into Alternative Investments

697 words
28 September 2016
18:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Malaysia's second-largest pension fund bought into ride-sharing service Uber Technologies Inc. last week, in a deal that marked a continuing shift in local funds' investment strategies. It was also the fund's first direct investment in a foreign company, reports Nikkei staff writer Ching Yee Choo.

Fresh from the annual Malaysian Private Equity Forum last week where it played host, Kumpulan Wang Persaraan, a $30 billion pension fund, spent $30 million on the deal, according to Reuters, quoting sources. Uber Technologies declined to comment on the sum.

The fund's latest investment signals a changing trend in local funds' allocation to include more alternative asset classes such as private equity. Currently, its investment allocation is at a ratio of 9:1, with the bulk invested in traditional asset classes such as fixed income and equities.

Other notable investments from local funds into alternative asset classes are Khazanah Nasional Berhad's investments in mobile entertainment provider Garena Interactive Holding Ltd. and augmented reality search engine Blippar Inc., as well as mobile lending and credit analytics platform company WeLab Holdings. Khazanah is the Malaysian government's investment arm.

As one of those affected by the foreign exchange volatility, KWAP announced that it would be making changes in its asset allocation in favor of alternative asset classes such as private equity. Its alternative asset-class investments consist of real estate at 7%, private equity at 2% and infrastructure at 1%.

KWAP also wants to participate in the technology and logistics sectors. This is in line with its intention to tweak its asset allocation in a "lower for longer investment climate," which affected its return on investment, a measure of the amount returned relative to investment costs.

According to its annual report, KWAP's ROI had been increasing year-over-year from 2011 onward but fell in 2014. In 2015, it fell short of its target ROI of 6%, achieving 5.4% ROI instead. It expected to post a 5% ROI this year.

The slowdown in global economic growth has affected deal making in the private-equity sector, with the number of transactions tapering in the past two years within Southeast Asia. But not all hope is lost, according to panelists from MPEF.

The global private-equity capital raised in 2015 at $527 billion was lower than the $555 billion recorded the previous year, according to Bain & Co.'s "Global Private Equity Report 2016." Recent data from alternative assets industry intelligence firm Preqin reveals that only fairly recently the global private-equity fundraising activities picked up, bringing the total funds raised to date to $182 billion, compared with $137 billion in the previous corresponding period.

In a global environment of lackluster growth stemming from low interest rates, high volatility and valuations, the forum shed light on private equity as an under tapped investment class that yields better returns than public equities.

On the venture-capital front, concerns abound that Malaysia and the Southeast Asia region is playing catch-up to the aggressively expansionary China.

Private-equity firms admit it is a tough market. Some said they look for the most interesting opportunities with an international angle, while others spoke of "permanently warm areas." The latter were described as a decadeslong trend that continues because of "natural tailwinds" in the industry, owing to the demographics and socioeconomics of a particular country or region.

For venture-capital firms, concerns were raised about the relatively slower pace of deals inked between local or regional venture-capital firms. "If Malaysia cannot provide for [startups], they will go out of the [country] to find [funds]," said 500 Startups Managing Partner Ng Khailee. They cited Malaysia's ride-sharing app firm Grab, which initially received local funding but last week clinched $750 million from a series F round led by Japan's SoftBank Group. Overall, venture-capital firms believe that the future is in financial technology, with consumers as drivers of growth.

The private-equity funds raised in Southeast Asia total about $5.7 billion, while the rest of Asia saw a total of $60 billion to date, according to the Asian Venture Capital Journal.

Quants Zero In On New Target

By Bradley Hope and Leslie Scism 
681 words
28 September 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Two Sigma Investments LP spent the past 15 years honing its quantitative approach to investing. Now, it is turning its number-crunching prowess on new prey: insurance.

The firm is joining with American International Group Inc. and Hamilton Insurance Group Ltd. to provide an automated, online analytical system to issue policies in minutes to smaller businesses like clothing shops, beauty parlors and medical offices.

Under the arrangement, agents will go online and type in the barest of information about their client -- business name and address and a few other details -- and algorithms will begin filling in information about the prospective policyholder. That is in contrast to longstanding practices in which insurers often ask dozens of questions for the process to get started.

For Two Sigma, it is the first significant attempt to branch out to a new business from investing. The firm is known as one of the most successful "quants," which are quantitative hedge funds that use computer systems to trawl data, make predictions and trade automatically in the markets.

Founded in 2001 by a computer scientist, David Siegel, and a mathematician, John Overdeck, who had both worked in hedge funds in the past, it increasingly is casting itself as the Google Inc. of Wall Street, a technology company that can take its data-crunching skills to work on a variety of finance and economics problems.

Last October, it hired Alfred Spector, Google's former vice president of research and special initiatives, to become its chief technology officer.

Two Sigma thinks of itself as a company that focuses on "problems involving risk and economic activity," rather than as a hedge fund, Mr. Siegel said. The insurance deal is its first major effort outside of the markets, but he said over time it plans to target other opportunities where it can use those skills.

Small and medium-size business insurance is a "gigantic market" and a "data science" problem, he said.

"It's not just about taking manual processes and automating them," Mr. Siegel said. "By using our data science, we think we can do a better job of underwriting."

The algorithms being used by the Two Sigma group's venture, called Attune, will draw on detail that Two Sigma has obtained from vendors who collect public records and other sources that can tell AIG and Hamilton what they believe they need to know to understand the risk to be insured.

That could include detail about square footage and construction features of the business premises, changes to mechanical systems over the years as outlined in government building permits, proximity to fire hydrants and sources of flooding, as well as code violations, other regulatory run-ins and legal entanglements, the firms said.

In most instances, the algorithms would be expected to assess the risk and determine a policy's price almost instantaneously.

The effort to speed up the issuance of policies isn't the first. In February, Warren Buffett's Berkshire Hathaway Inc. launched the online coveryourbusiness.com, where business owners can go directly to obtain policies.

"Every person is buying more and more online, even paper towels, so why not insurance?"said Rakesh Gupta, the Berkshire initiative's chief operating officer. Believing simplicity for the applicant is key, the Berkshire team is continuing to whittle down the number of questions traditionally asked by "questioning all the questions."

AIG Chief Executive Peter Hancock said he was attracted to the partnership by Two Sigma's data-analysis record. They have been "learning the hard lessons of what analytics can and can't do," he said.

AIG is well-known for covering complex risks at big companies. While it hasn't focused on basic property and liability policies for small enterprises, it does provide them workers-compensation insurance and specialized coverage to protect against such things as cyberrisk.

Bermuda-based Hamilton was established in late 2013 by a group of investors, led by insurance-industry veteran Brian Duperreault and including principals of Two Sigma.

Energy Funds Ask for More Time, Money

By Ryan Dezember and Dawn Lim 
1,104 words
28 September 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Rocked by the fall in oil-and-gas prices, some energy-focused private-equity funds are pleading with their investors for more time and money.

EnerVest Ltd., a Houston-based investment firm that says it operates more U.S. oil-and-gas wells than any other company, is asking investors in two of its funds to put up hundreds of millions of dollars to bolster the troubled vehicles or risk losing the billions they have already invested. "No question, this is a giant disappointment," Chief Executive John Walker said in an interview.

First Reserve Corp., meanwhile, is negotiating with investors to extend the life of a $7.8 billion fund from 2006 to give it time for oil prices to rebound, according to pension records and people familiar with the matter. The fund has lost more than a third of its original value, and First Reserve has proposed waiving management fees it charges investors in exchange for more time to sell the fund's remaining holdings, according to people familiar with the matter.

Private-equity firms were among the most avid financiers in the shale-drilling boom of the last decade, which helped produce market-flooding supplies of oil and gas. Two years ago, oil prices collapsed, and they have languished at about $45 a barrel. The decline has vexed investors across Wall Street who financed the shale boom with $100-a-barrel oil in mind.

The woes of private-equity energy funds are rippling to pensions and other institutional investors that pumped more than $400 billion into these pools since shale drilling took off. While prices could eventually rebound, bringing investments back to profitability, some private-equity funds don't have time to wait.

Funds raised a decade ago are proving particularly painful for investors. Funds typically have a limited lifespan, promising to sell their assets and return investors' money after 10 years. Many are filled with assets worth less than they cost and face the prospect of liquidating at losses. The alternatives are for investment firms to negotiate deals with their investors to extend the fund or bring in outside investors with fresh cash.

"There are no good options when an energy fund reaches the end of its life at the bottom of the cycle," said Jay Yoder, head of real assets at Pavilion Alternatives Group, a Sacramento, Calif., firm that advises private-equity investors. "The number of energy funds that need more time is at a level we've not seen before."

The price slump has left old funds reluctant to unload assets. At year-end, private-equity funds specializing in oil and gas that were raised between 2005 and 2007 held assets valued at $34.5 billion, or about half the $71.1 billion that those 131 funds started out with, according to data provider Preqin.

EnerVest built a network of 40,000 wells by pairing its investors' cash with borrowed money. Now, EnerVest's lenders, led by Wells Fargo & Co., are forcing it to repay a big chunk of the money borrowed by two funds because the oil-and-gas reserves used as collateral lost much of their value when energy prices collapsed. That has put the funds at risk of default.

Mr. Walker said he and other EnerVest executives put $90 million of their own toward repaying lenders, and some of the firm's investors have committed $250 million. But about $150 million is still needed.

EnerVest, which said it eliminated management fees on the older of the two funds and halved them for the other, is asking investors to contribute roughly 10%-12% of their original investments in the $1.5 billion and $2 billion funds.

"We have some limited partners that are mad as hell at us," said Mr. Walker, referring to the funds' investors. "We've had some chew us out and hang up on us."

In 2013, EnerVest borrowed $1.4 billion for its 13th fund. Mr. Walker said EnerVest paid back much of that debt, but the fund was still leveraged when oil prices plummeted in late 2014.

"We thought we were underleveraged," said Mr. Walker, a former Wall Street analyst who founded the firm in 1992. EnerVest's funds have typically returned more than 30%, and the firm raised $2.4 billion in November for its 14th and largest-ever fund.

But investors in its 2010 and 2013 funds, such as Orange County Employees Retirement System, face potentially large losses. Ocers records show that at year-end, $54 million of investments in those funds had been written down to $9.7 million.

"There is not a practical way for Ocers to exit its investments without taking a material pricing haircut," the pension's investment chief wrote to its board. "We will most likely ride out this horse."

That is what First Reserve is asking investors to do after a plan to restructure its troubled fund by moving assets into a new fund fizzled when too few investors agreed to sell their stakes.

First Reserve invested more than half its $7.8 billion fund in the run-up to more than $140-a-barrel oil in 2008, according to private-markets analytics firm Bison. Since then, the fund has lost roughly 35% of investors' cash, or about $2.7 billion, through June 30, according to public pension data.

Now, the Greenwich, Conn., firm is trying to extend the fund's life.

Earlier this year, California Public Employees' Retirement System pushed for a break on fees charged by First Reserve to manage the remaining investments, much of which are shares in an offshore oil explorer and pipeline company that have both lost more than 90% over the last two years.

In June, Real Desrochers, Calpers's head of private-equity investing, wrote First Reserve Chairman and co-CEO Bill Macaulay and suggested the firm distribute the shares to investors, according to correspondence reviewed by The Wall Street Journal. That would eliminate management fees charged for holding the stock. First Reserve has reaped more than $500 million in fees since the fund was launched, Mr. Desrochers wrote.

Mr. Macaulay responded that First Reserve has offered ways for investors to sell out of the fund "or remain invested in a fund that will have a sufficient duration to benefit from the recovery in the energy markets." Since then, First Reserve has told some investors that it would waive management fees if the fund's life is extended, according to people familiar with the discussions.

Markets
Battle Over Munis Moves to Senate; Bill set to be introduced this week calls for including municipal bonds in bank-safety rules

By Andrew Ackerman 
779 words
27 September 2016
15:27
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications:

An aide to Sen. Richard Shelby (R., Ala.), chairman of the Senate Banking Committee, said he wouldn't object to the coming municipal bond legislation. An earlier version of this story said an aide to Mr. Shelby said he would support the bill. Also, these comments were made by an aide to Sen. Shelby. Due to an editing mistake, an earlier correction to this story erroneously cited Sen. Shelby for these remarks. 

WASHINGTON A bipartisan group of senators is pushing to include municipal bonds in bank-safety rules, the latest wrinkle in a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/washington-battles-over-munis-safe-or-hard-to-sell-1456962547" over how safe and salable the debt of states and localities would be in another financial crisis.

Sens. Mark Warner (D., Va.), Charles Schumer (D., N.Y.) and Mike Rounds (R., S.D.) are set to introduce legislation on municipal bonds this week, according to Senate aides. The bill aims to open the door for big U.S. banks to count municipal bonds as liquid assets under rules completed in 2014 that were designed to ensure Wall Street firms have enough cash during a crisis to fund their operations for 30 days.

The Senate legislation would place municipal bonds on the lowest rung of the "high quality liquid assets" category. That means they would be treated on par with corporate bonds, but not as favorably as under related legislation approved by the House early this year.

"We must ensure a continued and reliable access to capital markets for our local governments," Mr. Warner said in a written statement. "This legislation represents a compromise that achieves that while appropriately balancing concerns for the long term stability of our financial system."

The rules, slated to go into effect next year, are aimed at making banks hold more cash or securities that are easy to sell. The Federal Reserve and two other bank regulators had originally decided debt issued by states and localities didn't make the cut prompting a backlash from banks, lawmakers and states and localities who warned the move would make the bonds less attractive and raise borrowing costs for municipalities.

The Fed completed amendments in April to allow some investment-grade municipal bonds to qualify. But the two other regulators involved in the rules the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency and the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp . haven't followed suit.

Aides to Senate lawmakers say their bill was scaled back from the House version to gain broad support for it in the Senate, though it is unclear if there is sufficient time in the remaining year to advance the bill.

Sen. Richard Shelby (R., Ala.), chairman of the Senate Banking Committee, indicated earlier this year that he was reluctant to second-guess banking regulators that originally excluded municipal bonds when they wrote the rules in 2014. But an aide to Mr. Shelby said he wouldn't object to the coming bill as it incorporates changes the Fed already adopted in its version of the rules.

Banks underwrite muni bonds, buy them as investments and sell them to clients. Lenders have played an increasingly central role in the thinly traded, $3.7 trillion market and are now the biggest buyers of municipal debt, according to Municipal Market Analytics Inc., a research firm.

Municipal officials have generally applauded the Fed's willingness to make changes to the rules but say legislation is necessary, largely because banking firms typically hold municipal debt in units that are overseen by the other policy makers involved in the rules, particularly the OCC , which regulates national banks.

Officials at the OCC remain dismissive of including the municipal bonds in the rules and don't believe the debt is sufficiently liquid, according to people familiar with their thinking. The FDIC is waiting until the rules go into effect next year before considering amending its version, according to people familiar with that agency.

While the Senate bill would rank municipal debt similarly to the Fed's amended rules allowing the banks to count 50% of the bonds' face value when including them in their funding buffers the legislation would allow banks to include more types of municipal bonds, a Senate aide said.

These include revenue bonds, or securities backed by a specific revenue stream, that comprise the bulk of debt issued by states and local governments but that are kept out of the current Fed version of the rules.

The House bill, meanwhile, is broader than both the Senate bill and the Fed's version of the rules, allowing banks to count 85% of the bonds' face value.

To date, banks have by and large continued to hold lots of municipal bonds despite the rules, in part because they are seen as less risky than corporate debt and are priced competitively to other types of debt, according to bank officials. If interest rates rise this year, banks are expected to begin to pare their muni holdings.

SMART MONEY
BoJ wakes up to the pain of negative rates felt by Japanese banks

By Henny Sender 
723 words
27 September 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
22
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

There were plenty of warning signs that the Bank of Japan's policy of negative interest rates was doomed.

The first sign was a rise in the yen in January, when the policy was introduced. It was both unexpected and unwanted since a handful of exporters such as Toyota, which benefit from a cheap currency, have been a significant source of growth for decades.

Then there were also sounds of distress from GPIF, the Japanese pension fund, and Japan Postal Savings. They feared that the prices of financial assets were increasingly artificial thanks to central bank policies. Invest today and lose tomorrow when the stimulus policies cease or reverse.

By April, even the head of the Financial Services Agency, Nobuchika Mori, delicately criticised the BoJ action at a speech at the annual ISDA meeting. However, it was only when the chief executive of Mitsubishi UFJ, Nobuyuki Hirano , joined in the chorus of naysayers and quit the government's primary dealership , that it became clear the BoJ began to take notice. The central bank announced a review of its actions, which culminated in last week's decision not to push interest rates further into negative territory .

Across all markets that central banks have been engaged in unconventional monetary policies, there have been many victims in the financial community. They include pension funds, insurers and asset managers, as well as ordinary households hoping to earn something on their savings but that don't have access to the leveraged opportunities of the wealthy. Yet it was not until the banks started feeling the pain that central banks seemed to reconsider.

It is interesting, albeit somewhat puzzling, to note banks' clout, which contrasts markedly with that of others in finance. And the muscle of banks persists, despite them mattering less and less.

Especially in Japan, banks still depend on the gap between the short-term rates at which they borrow and the long-term rates at which they lend for their profits. The Bank of Japan's latest measures, announced last week, are intended to steepen the yield curve and address banks' anxieties about their after tax profits. That is especially true of Japan's many smaller regional banks - the shares of which foreign fund managers are now shorting.

There is a similar dynamic elsewhere. Virtually all those who have been hit by central bank policies have, eight years after the crisis that gave rise to them, been reluctant to point out that they have proved ineffective and costly.

One of the few insurers to speak out and destroy the myths has been Swiss Re. Indeed, 18 months ago it attempted to quantify the costs in foregone income to both US savers and European insurers as a result of the respective central bank policies on both sides of the Atlantic.

That is why earnings of US insurers have dropped (well before those of the banks). For example, Metropolitan Life warned in its most recent quarterly results that it needed to bolster its reserves by $2bn largely because of a squeeze from low interest rates. It is also why a recent report from Morgan Stanley flags the difficulties faced by active fund management firms. "Low growth and low rates weigh on active manager performance, placing significant pressure on fees," the report notes.

In the US, one reason insurers are so reluctant to criticise the policies of the Federal Reserve, analysts say, is that the insurance industry currently falls under the purview of local state regulators. "They fear above all else coming under the much more critical eye of a national regulator such as the Fed," says one prominent investor.

Meanwhile, the plight of their clients, average Americans, continues to worsen, and as they age, their standard of living will drop further.

"It is thus now impossible for retirees to ensure quality of life and others to save for secure retirement through the deposit and investment options suitable for and available to low/modest-income households," argues Karen Petrou, managing partner of Federal Financial Analytics. "Current income-distribution distortions are thus likely only to get worse faster as savings fall into ever deeper holes. Contemplate rising inflation without rising savings returns and be particularly afraid." Indeed.

Markets
IHS Markit Swings to Loss on Deal Costs; London-based data and analytics  firm created from a merger in July

By Austen Hufford

27 September 2016

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Fresh off completing a merger between U.K.-based, financial-data provider Markit and U.S.-based information and analytics provider IHS , IHS Markit Ltd . said Tuesday that revenue rose but profit swung to a loss due to the deal's costs.

The "http://www.wsj.com/articles/ihs-and-markit-to-merge-creating-data-heavyweight-1458556990" and completed in July.

London-based IHS Markit feeds data and analytics to energy, financial, transportation and design companies with information on oil and gas fields, mineral rights, derivatives, currencies, loans and supply-chain forecasts and analysis.

"We are off to a strong start with our integration efforts, giving us confidence in achieving our synergy targets," Chief Executive Jerre Stead said.

During the quarter, the company took $104.5 million in costs related to acquisitions, including the deal.

The company said organic revenue which strips out the impact of acquisitions and currency swings fell 2% as organic revenue fell in the resources and design segments but was partially offset by growth in transportation and financial services.

The data provider said it expects revenue for the year ending in November of between $2.74 billion and $2.77 billion and adjusted earnings per share of between $1.72 and $1.78. Analysts were expecting earnings per share of $1.75 on revenue of $2.75 billion.

Overall for the quarter ended August 31, Markit reported a loss of $31.7 million, or 9 cents a share, compared with a profit of $59.1 million, or 24 cents a share a year prior. Excluding charges and other special items, earnings were 45 cents a share.

Revenue grew 30% to $724.6 million, primarily through acquisitions.

Analysts surveyed by Thomson Reuters expected 44 cents a share in earnings on $727.5 million in revenue.

Why Rent Office Space When You Can Buy a Whole Building? Mainland Chinese companies fuel surge in tower-buying in Hong Kong, hoping to raise their international profiles

By Anjie Zheng 
842 words
27 September 2016
19:48
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

HONG KONG As demand from mainland China sends office rents in Hong Kong soaring, some companies are choosing a higher-ticket strategy: buying entire skyscrapers.

Mainland Chinese companies have already spent $3.65 billion buying office space in Hong Kong this year, more than half the $6.31 billion they had spent in total since 2009, according to data from Real Capital Analytics.

Most recently, Cheung Kei Group, a private investment firm based in the neighboring Chinese megacity of Shenzhen, spent US$580.1 million in July to buy One HarbourGate's East Tower on the seafront in Kowloon, the northern part of Hong Kong. Last November, China's largest insurance company, China Life Insurance, bought the West Tower for $754.1 million.

The surge in tower-buying from the mainland is one way around Hong Kong's sky-high rents, the priciest in the world. At an annual $278.50 a square foot for prime office space, rental rates are on average nearly 80% higher in Hong Kong than in Manhattan, according to an annual ranking by property consultancy Knight Frank.

Brokers say another aim for many Chinese companies looking to expand in Hong Kong is to find a flashy address in prestigious locales. Many companies want buildings on which they can "put a big, shiny sign," said Rhodri James, executive director of offices at real-estate brokerage CBRE Group. China Life, for example, has already renamed the One HarbourGate's West Tower as the China Life Center.

Chinese interest has pushed up Hong Kong building prices to the point that they are rarely attractive investment opportunities to other companies, said Denis Ma, head of research at Jones Lang LaSalle in Hong Kong.

The flurry of buying by mainland investors has pushed the yields, or the amount made on rents divided by the price of a building, down to 2.5% to 3% from 4% just four years ago, said Mr. Ma.

Hong Kong, a part of China that still operates under a separate legal system, remains the top destination for mainland Chinese investors looking to buy commercial property abroad. Chinese companies have spent $3.65 billion on commercial property here since January, compared with $2.94 billion in New York and $1.48 billion in London, according to Real Capital Analytics.

Major Chinese companies have owned prominent buildings in the city's main financial district for several years. At night, neon signs on the China Construction Bank and the Agricultural Bank of China towers, owned by two of China's biggest banks, shine near the knifelike Bank of China building, designed by I.M. Pei and one of the city's most iconic structures.

The latest wave of Chinese buyers say Hong Kong property can help build their international profile.

Chinese builder Evergrande Real Estate said its US$1.61 billion purchase of the Mass Mutual Tower last November would "drastically enhance the group's corporate image in Hong Kong as well as international markets, which could significantly boost the group's brand promotion."

The deal for the tower, which closed in January, is the second-most expensive Chinese purchase of commercial property globally this year, behind China Life Insurance and partners' purchase of New York's PaineWebber Building for $1.65 billion in May, according to Real Capital Analytics.

The desire among Chinese investors for Hong Kong buildings comes as big Chinese real-estate developers also are "http://www.wsj.com/articles/big-chinese-developers-push-into-hong-kong-market-1468938361" of public land in the city.

Closer financial ties are also bringing in more Chinese companies. Hong Kong's stock exchange already has a trading link with the exchange in Shanghai, and a similar link with the Shenzhen market is due to open later this year.

While Chinese companies still prefer to buy in Hong Kong's central business district, their desire to own is so great and supply so scarce that some will settle for high-profile buildings elsewhere in the city.

Asset manager China Everbright Ltd. bought the Dah Sing Financial Center in February for US$1.29 billion in Wan Chai, a secondary business district, to use as its Hong Kong headquarters. The company said it will mainly use the building itself, as business in Hong Kong is growing fast, and "is considering" renaming the building.

One stumbling block for prospective Chinese buyers is the lack of buildings on sale. Despite the surge in Chinese buying, the commercial property market here isn't as liquid as those in London or New York, said Mr. James of CBRE.

"A lot of the Hong Kong developers are here for the long term, and won't change their strategy by putting their core buildings up for sale," he said.

Pro Private Markets
SAP Acquires Big-Data  Specialist Altiscale

By Deborah Gage 
339 words
27 September 2016
18:59
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Altiscale Inc., one of the first startups to offer the big-data software Hadoop as an online service, said it has been acquired by SAP SE. Terms of the deal weren't disclosed.

"Early on we naively thought the biggest problem a company had was an infrastructure problem, running Hadoop at scale," said founder and Chief Executive Raymie Stata. " &But if you look at the journey from new data sources available to transformative changes in business processes, it was a lot longer journey than we assumed."

Altiscale had raised about $42 million from Sequoia Capital, General Catalyst Partners, Northgate, Accel Partners, AME Cloud Ventures, Boston Seed Capital and angel investors.

Mr. Stata, a former chief technology officer and chief architect at Yahoo Inc. where Hadoop was first developed, founded Altiscale in 2012 with the idea that the startup could take away some the complexity of using Hadoop. But the company had trouble growing.

Altiscale considered raising more funding, and about a year ago, in an effort to jump-start its business, introduced a software layer on top of Hadoop to help customers analyze their data. The company was also developing analytic templates for customers in vertical markets like financial services and health care.

But SAP was "way ahead of us on both scores," he said.

Altiscale will become a business unit of SAP, and Mr. Stata said he felt good about the outcome. "More often than not you fail outright" he said. "...Only once in a while do companies make it all the way through to an IPO."

Other Hadoop companies have also struggled as the market for big-data software proves harder to crack than investors first assumed. Hortonworks Inc. has seen its market cap cut in half, to about $500 million since its public debut in 2014. MapR Technologies Inc., which is still private, raised a down round in August and has since named a new CEO, with founder and former CEO John Schroeder becoming executive chairman.

Genetic-Testing Startup Color Genomics Closes $45M Series B

By Brian Gormley 
354 words
27 September 2016

The Wall Street Journal Online

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Color Genomics Inc., which has introduced low-cost genetic tests for cancer in an effort to make them easily accessible, has raised $45 million in Series B financing.

General Catalyst Partners is leading the round, which included existing investors Susan L. Wagner, pop singer Bono, 8VC, Emerson Collective and Khosla Ventures. Color Genomics, formed in 2013 and launched two years later, has raised $60 million in total. Ms. Wagner is co-founder of asset-management firm BlackRock Inc.

Color's product, which physicians order for patients, is a 30-gene test for hereditary forms of cancer, including breast, colorectal, prostate, stomach and uterine cancers. The company focuses on high-impact genes that, when mutated, dramatically increase the risk of developing cancer, according to co-founder and Chief Executive Elad Gil.

Burlingame, Calif.-based "https://getcolor.com/" uses software and machine learning to increase quality, lower costs and remove human error from genetic testing, according to Dr. Gil. Its 30-gene test sells for $249; some genetic tests that sell for thousands of dollars, he said. Although not covered by insurance, Color tests are affordable to many people, and the company is trying make them more accessible to those at particularly high risk for cancers with hereditary risk.

Color is collaborating with the BRCA Foundation and private donors to offer genetic testing to parents, siblings and adult children of people with gene mutations for $50. Some companies also offer Color tests as a benefit, including Visa Inc., the Sacramento Kings, Andreessen Horowitz and Y Combinator, Dr. Gil said.

Color, which operates in a market with large companies such as Myriad Genetics Inc. and smaller players like Invitae Corp., also collaborates with cancer researchers. This includes an alliance with the University of California system on a study to determine if a personalized approach to breast cancer screening based on a person's risk profile is as effective as annual mammograms.

Ms. Wagner and General Catalyst Managing Director Hemant Taneja are joining the board.

Heard on The Street
Looming Storm in Insurance

By Paul J. Davies 
493 words
27 September 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The insurance industry is in a tight spot. This is most obvious in property reinsurance, but that is one symptom of a squeeze across the sector.

Global catastrophe reinsurance prices fell this year below levels last seen in 2001 before the destruction of the World Trade Center, according to Guy Carpenter, a risk-and-reinsurance specialist owned by brokers Marsh & McLennan.

The question for property-and-casualty insurers isn't only whether reinsurance pricing will find a floor, but how much the industry is starting to amplify financial and actuarial risks now.

Ultralow investment returns are hurting profits while drawing a wider range of yield-hungry investors into becoming suppliers of alternative capital to underwrite reinsurance risks. At the same time, the lack of costly disasters in recent years has helped a surfeit of capital build up, making it cheap.

The drop in prices is slowing, according to Moody's, but they are still declining. Similarly, the growth of alternative capital in catastrophe bonds and other vehicles is slowing, but it isn't going away.

And while some see these trends as early indicators that reinsurance prices may find a floor, there is another problem: Demand for reinsurance from ordinary insurers has continued to slow, too. That is because ordinary property insurance is suffering low demand itself. Insurance demand is linked to economic activity, which globally has been sluggish.

But as well as slower cyclical growth in many markets, ordinary insurers are also facing longer-term changes that might permanently cut the amount of insurance people and firms need, such as better risk management or use of big data. Insurers' options to improve returns to shareholders then are few. One is to take on more insurance risk. Another is to increase balance-sheet leverage.

Reinsurers are writing policies with higher probable maximum losses, according to Moody's, especially those linked to U.S. hurricanes. The largest loss reinsurers expect from a one-in-250-year-size U.S. hurricane event is equivalent to 16% of their equity this year, on average, up almost 2 percentage points from last year, leaving reinsurers more exposed to natural catastrophe despite lower pricing, Moody's said.

There also are fears that ordinary insurers will become more aggressive with reserving policies, putting aside less money to cover expected claims, to boost underwriting profits, according to CreditSights, a research firm. That will hurt tomorrow's profits if they underestimate today's claims.

Finally, reinsurers hunting for new business are trying to sell more structured policies designed to release capital from ordinary insurers' balance sheets, which can fund shareholder payouts or investments elsewhere. That risks concentrating the industry's capital safety net in fewer hands and adding leverage to the system as a whole. Left unchecked, increasing leverage and higher risk could see the industry itself heading for catastrophe.
