Economy
Transcript of Federal Reserve Governor Lael Brainard's Comments; Ms. Brainard answered questions after delivering a speech on the economy and monetary policy in Chicago

6,329 words
12 September 2016
22:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Federal Reserve Governor Lael Brainard answered questions Monday after "http://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-lael-brainard-case-to-tighten-policy-preemptively-is-less-compelling-1473701143" on the economy and monetary policy to The Chicago Council on Global Affairs.

Here is a transcript of her remarks during the Q&A session, lightly edited for length and clarity.

MICHAEL H. MOSKOW: All right. We're all set. Lael, thank you so much for those comments. Very thoughtful speech. Gives us all a great deal to think about.

So let me as you know, we'll have a little discussion here and then we'll open up for questions from the audience. Let me start with the forecast, because people may not realize it but the ability of the Fed to forecast the economy is extremely important, because when you formulate monetary policy you're looking ahead, not in the past. And you got to decide whether to influence monetary policy in the future. Well, the Fed forecast, as well as a lot of private sector forecasters, have been consistently off in for the last five, six years. They've over-forecasted output. They've under-forecasted the rate at which the unemployment rate would come down. And they've over-forecasted inflation as well. And as I said, it's not just the Fed, but private sector forecasters too. Why do you think this is?

LAEL BRAINARD: Well, so I think over the course of the recovery there are a variety of reasons that the actual economy has underperformed the forecast on aggregate growth. The first is, of course, that the financial crisis was extraordinarily deep, and that it was a period when households experienced very severe losses in their balance sheets. And it has taken a long time for households to rebuild some economic security, for credit conditions to turn to more normal. So that is one of the reasons, is that the return from very deep financial crises often gets slower relative to other kinds of cyclical recoveries.

MR. MOSKOW: Slower than forecasted?

MS. BRAINARD: Slower than forecasted, relative to previous recoveries.

Secondly, there have been a series of very significant foreign shocks that had not been anticipated in some of those earlier forecasts and, indeed, have not been anticipated in subsequent forecasts as well. Japan, as I stated earlier, is extremely weakened, has been dealing with some of its own challenges. The euro area has experienced a number of periods of extreme financial volatility and has undergone a financial crisis that has taken a different path than in the United States. And of course, the source of strength that was previously emerging market growth has also most recently been a source of volatility and downward drag on the U.S. And I described that in some detail, but of course China has been a big factor most recently. So those are the first two things.

But third, potential growth has also come in consistently below what was anticipated. So if you look at (Congressional Budget Office) numbers on potential growth, it has declined from about 2.6% (annually), according to CBO estimates prior to the crisis, to about 1.4% subsequent to the crisis. And that in turn reflects a few things first of all, labor market slowing that was greater than anticipated. Now, obviously, some of the retirement of the baby boom generation was well-anticipated, but in fact participation rates dropped more, particularly among prime-age workers, than had been anticipated. And again, this is the reason that there may be more slack there than we had anticipated. And secondly, productivity growth has been much lower than was anticipated. And there, there are a variety of explanations that have been given, but I don't think anybody fully understands that phenomenon yet.

MR. MOSKOW: OK, so this potential growth was a very key concept because, as you said, it reflects the growth of the labor force and the growth of productivity at the same time. So just on the labor force side, you mentioned in your comments that you think there's room for further there's further slack here. But actually, there are a couple of indicators that show that the labor market's really quite tight now. The unemployment rate's 4.9%. But job openings are extremely high now. The number of unemployed people per job opening is very low now. And as you said, the participation rate for prime-age males is low. Why is that? What's happening here? Do you have a can you help us understand these apparent contradictions here?

MS. BRAINARD: So I think, you know, if we if we perfectly understood the reasons for the lower prime-age labor participation rate I think that would help us a great deal in being much more precise about what the estimate of natural the natural rate of unemployment is. But of course, we don't we don't know that with certainty. What we do know is that the rate of labor-force participation has been quite elevated and for the prime-age workers quite elevated throughout the recovery. And it's really only very recently that we've seen a half-percentage-point increase in that prime-age labor-force participation rate, which I think is a positive development.

And yet, even with that, we're 1  percentage points below where we were pre-crisis. Now, it may be that it takes a while after a very scarring downturn for people to be brought back into the labor force. And in fact, that's part of the reason that we've seen the unemployment rate moving sideways, as the jobs numbers have been quite robust in the recent months, is because people are coming in who had left the labor force and are coming back in. And it may simply be that it took a while for the labor market to be sufficiently promising for some of those former workers to be brought back in.

As we were noting earlier, wage inflation is really very subdued. It's really only picked up to the 2 % level, which is well-below what one would have expected pre-crisis. Job openings, as you say, have been elevated. But there are different kinds of ways that people post job openings. And so there are a lot of speculation around whether that number is the same number as we may have been looking at pre-crisis. And how do we compare those? So it's a kind of rich data picture which, at least in my case, leads me to conclude that there may well be additional slack, and that given the variety of reasons that we will expect inflation pressures to be quite moderate and slow, that that gives us some space to continue reducing the slack in the labor market.

MR. MOSKOW: And on the participation rate side, of course, we had a real ramp up in female participation, which is very positive, and then leveled off. But male participation has had a long-term steady decline. Have you any thoughts as to why that's occurring?

MS. BRAINARD: So I you know, I talk to a lot of labor market economists about that question. And I you know, I can't offer any particular pieces of research that you're not well-familiar with. But again, my sense is that there may well be a part of that that is a response to a deeply scarring financial crisis, and that we still have some room to go in drawing some of the prime-age labor force male and female back into work. And of course, that matters, not just for growth, but that matters for the long-term productive potential of the economy, as well as for the well-being of those individuals and their families.

MR. MOSKOW: Right. Now, it's very important and of course, the decline in male participation goes back way before the financial crisis. It's a long-term trend that no one can seem to explain. The other part of potential growth that you referred to, of course, is productivity growth, which is so important to raising our standard of living the amount of output per unit of input or per worker. And you mentioned you didn't have any good you hadn't been satisfied with the types of explanations you've heard as to why productivity growth has slowed. But have you any thoughts as to what could be done at this point to increase productivity growth?

MS. BRAINARD: Well, so the numbers there are really very stark you know, upwards of 2% productivity growth in the 50 years right up to 2000, and now since the crisis, we have in the last five years we have only half a percentage point per year, one percentage point if you look over 10 years. There are a variety of hypotheses out there and a lot of interesting research.

You know, there is a hypothesis that we had very elevated productivity growth for a very short period of time, and that that innovation has now been fully absorbed, and therefore this is a return to sort of pre-'90s normal. That's one hypothesis.

Another hypothesis is that, for a variety of reasons, in part because of the or primarily because of the financial crisis, that credit conditions were particularly daunting. For a period of time the rate of business startups fell considerably, and the adoption of some of the very promising technologies has been very slow. So there are good technologies out there, according to that story, but that the adoption of them, you know, has been slowed by these factors. You know, people point to artificial intelligence, for instance, or robotics. In that latter set of explanations, you would actually expect to see productivity starting to rise again as business dynamism increases because credit conditions are better and adoption is more widespread.

I will say the only other piece of information that seems important, as well, is that this phenomena is not confined to the United States; that productivity growth has slowed globally, and in fact more so in other parts of the world.

In terms of the kinds of things that policy makers can do, there are probably very important things that sit with Congress and the executive branch having to do with a whole host of policies infrastructure investment, R&D, education, worker training. But again, those are policies that really sit with the executive branch and Congress, state and local governments in some cases.

MR. MOSKOW: Would you add government regulation to that list, too?

MS. BRAINARD: Absolutely. More efficient and effective regulation is also part of that list.

MR. MOSKOW: What about business investment? That's a key ingredient here, too, in increasing productivity, and business investment has been incredibly slow, even negative, in some quarters for about a year now. Any thoughts as to why this is occurring?

MS. BRAINARD: So one of the factors that clearly has been weighing on business investment at least for the past three quarters when, as you've said, we've seen negative business investment has been the run-up in the exchange rate. Another factor that was important early on in that period was the sharp decrease in the price of oil. We saw very sharp drops in drilling and investment in mining. And we have subsequently seen business investment more broadly being impacted. You see it on corporate profitability, particularly of businesses that are internationally oriented. And you see it particularly in the manufacturing sector. So clearly some of the forces that have been weighing on business investment have been global in nature, but there's still a question about having a more supportive environment for business investment domestically. And again, I'm hoping to see some of those numbers looking better in the third quarter.

MR. MOSKOW: We all hope they look better. (Chuckles.)

Let me shift a bit to financial stability, which is an important concern of the-- of the Fed, along with its other objectives of maximum growth and price stability. But (Fed Chairwoman) Janet Yellen said recently that keeping interest rates at zero for an extended period of time might encourage excessive risk-taking and undermine financial stability. So doesn't this risk increase every year that interest rates are so low? And how do you balance this risk against the objectives which you've talked about, about growth and price stability?

MS. BRAINARD: Yeah. So I think in a low neutral rate environment, one of the issues that will be with us for some time is this question of reach-for-yield behavior and whether we will see some development of financial imbalances with potential stability risks.

Now, we at the Board at the Federal Reserve Board have, in the wake of the crisis, put in place a much more systematic set of mechanisms, both to monitor some of these kinds of financial stability risks with a new division that is devoted to that, with a quarterly assessment which systematically looks at places where asset valuations might be out of line with historical norms, where leverage might be building, the kinds of that we saw going into the crisis that should have been flashing yellow. And in addition, we have a whole set of strengthened authorities to deal with the potential for asset valuations to get out of whack, and for leverage and maturity transformation to start to build. Obviously, in our supervisory tool kit, apart from the leverage ratio and massively enhanced capital requirements, liquidity requirements, we also have what I think of as extraordinarily useful stress tests that in particular focus on the largest institutions that might pose systemic risks, that are core to the system, and really runs them through very vigorous forward-looking scenarios, which and asks them to hold capital and liquidity against those scenarios. But we also have now a countercyclical buffer, and the guidance on that went out last week or we have the framework to put in place, if needed, a countercyclical capital buffer. So we've got a whole host of augmented authorities.

And you know, we have, in fact, when we've seen some excesses building, we've issued guidance and we've seen some impact there. So if you look at the leveraged lending guidance that we put out in 2013, subsequent to that we actually did see underwriting standards tighten in the supervised banking system. And more recently, at the end of last year, we put out guidance because we were concerned about some signs of building excesses in the commercial real-estate sector. And there, too, we've already seen in some of our lending surveys that underwriting standards have tightened.

But to come back to your earlier question, absolutely, in a low neutral rate environment we have to be attentive to the potential for these risks. On the other hand, the kind of low-inflation, low-growth, low-expectations environment that I was describing that some other countries have been grappling with is also an environment that is perilous for financial stability. So it's very important for us to avoid that as well.

MR. MOSKOW: But the longer you keep I don't want to put words in your mouth, but the longer you keep interest rates so low, isn't the risk greater?

MS. BRAINARD: I think the real question here is, if you're in an environment where the neutral rate is likely to remain low, and persistently so, that those kinds of reach-for-yield behaviors are ones that you need to be extra-attentive to.

MR. MOSKOW: Mmm hmm. One area is commercial real estate. A number of people have expressed concern about excesses in commercial real estate. Do you have any thoughts on that?

MS. BRAINARD: So we have in our surveillance exercises, that was a risk that we were picking up. We were seeing a lot of activity in that. Not as leveraged as you would have seen going into the crisis, but certainly the capitalization ratios there were of some concern. And so we did issue some guidance at the end of last year, obviously, to the institutions that we supervise in collaboration with the other bank regulators, and we've begun to see already that underwriting standards have tightened in response to that guidance. Again, it's within the supervised banking sector, but we have started to see some response already to that guidance. And we are going to keep a very close eye on those developments.

MR. MOSKOW: So you're monitoring it carefully.

MS. BRAINARD: Very carefully.

MR. MOSKOW: OK. Just thinking of the tools that central bankers are using these days, they're really extraordinarily different than 10 years ago. You have the (European Central Bank) is buying corporate bonds now. You've got the Bank of Japan is buying equities. The Fed now has a $4 trillion balance sheet, probably four times higher than it would have been under normal circumstances.

You're turning over to the Treasury about a hundred billion dollars a year, that's real money, a hundred billion (dollars) compared to maybe 20 or 25 (billion dollars) in earlier years.

Are you concerned about the unintended consequences of these extraordinary actions by these central banks?

MS. BRAINARD: So certainly the unconventional policies, asset purchases and forward guidance, that were used by the Federal Reserve, you know, we have closely studied them. There's been a lot of research. And the research, I think, concludes that they work through much the same channels as conventional monetary policy in terms of reducing interest rates and helping to support credit and aggregate demand generally.

But it is, of course, true that unconventional policies as well as some of the policies that have been undertaken by foreign central banks, that the Federal Reserve has not engaged in, you know, they're less well-tested, they're, therefore, less well-understood.

And so I think in the U.S. context there was a careful consideration of both the benefits and the potential risks. And I'm sure that is also true in other countries, as against, of course, the benefits and costs of not taking all the actions necessary to provide a robust response to the recovery, which also would, I think, have created some very considerable costs and risks.

So I think, you know, the research will continue. And, you know, as I stated earlier, it is precisely because we better understand our conventional tool kit, we have a lot more experience with it, we have much more precision around the impacts.

That leads me to be somewhat asymmetric when it comes to risk management in recognizing that we have more space using conventional monetary policy to respond to surprises associated with stronger-than-expected demand than we do with weaker-than-expected demand. And so that leads me to want to be very prudent.

MR. MOSKOW: Let me ask you one last question and then we'll open it up to questions from the audience.

You talked in your speech, you mentioned the importance of growth in foreign countries, or lack thereof, in terms of its impact on the U.S. economy, and you pointed to Europe and Japan, the slowdown in China and so on.

What about looking at it the other way? Do you think that the Fed should consider the impact of its decisions on other countries?

MS. BRAINARD: So we operate under very clear statutory guidelines given to us by Congress. Our dual mandate is to focus on U.S. maximum employment and U.S. price stability, which has been defined by the committee as 2% inflation.

So our focus has to be here on employment and inflation in the United States, but it is true that the United States operates in a global environment with currency that is, you know, valued as a safe haven, kind of an assets. We're in highly globalized financial markets.

And we have seen over the last few years that weakness abroad, adverse developments abroad really matter to the outlook here in the United States. The responsiveness of our exchange rate seems to be several times greater than it has been previously, and the impact on the U.S. flows through financial conditions, flows through on economic activity, not just net exports, although net exports have been hurt by the stronger dollar and we're starting to see that turn around a bit, but also on business investment and, of course, also on core inflation.

So when we are formulating policy to achieve the congressionally mandated goals of U.S. maximum employment, U.S. 2% inflation, we have to take into account weakness and risks from abroad.

MR. MOSKOW: OK. So we'll now open it up to questions from the floor. We have some people with microphones. And I would just ask you, please stand up and ask your question and identify yourself.

So we have a question right here.

Q: Thank you. Lyric Hughes Hale with EconVue.

My question, first of all, on the persistence of prime-age labor, the weakness there, there's a new report out by Chicago Booth that explains that it seems to be more sociological, that prime-age men are staying at home, and there's no amount of economic incentives that will make them change. So it's just a very interesting background.

Also, I read yesterday a report by the World Economic Forum on price inflation in the United States. And it said that really the United States is bifurcated in inflation. The things that we absolutely need to have, like health care and education, that those prices have been steadily going up since 1996, but we're insulated from the effect of those price rises because of student loans, because of public and private insurance for health care, and that the things we don't really need, like electronics, because of the strong dollar and because of China, are very cheap and that that's skewing the picture.

And it was something that I thought was intriguing. And I wonder if you've seen it and what you thought. Thank you.

MS. BRAINARD: No, both reports sound very interesting. I have not seen either, but they both sound interesting, and I will make sure to take a look. Yeah, thank you.

MR. MOSKOW: OK. Thank you.

Yes, right in the middle there's a we're getting you a microphone.

Q: Hi. I'm Rob Laragh from JHL Capital Group.

You mentioned in your speech a kind of correspondence between an approximate move in the dollar and an approximate amount of rate hikes. And I wonder if you could talk about how Fed policy has evolved to manage to kind of general financial conditions instead of just the funds rate.

And then also, what you think of, effectively, if you do that, we're much easier now than we were in December, you know, credit spreads are narrower, the dollar is weaker, equity prices are a little higher. What are your thoughts on that?

MS. BRAINARD: So certainly, the forecasting models that the Federal Reserve Board uses, both the international model and the U.S. model, do take into account financial conditions and how they impact activity as well as directly the effect of the exchange rate on the core imports part of inflation.

So it's possible to simulate an equivalence between how much exchange rate movement would be equivalent to the same amount in the models tightening in the federal funds rate in terms of their impact on activity and in terms of their impact on inflation. And that's where you get the 200 basis point estimate from.

But of course, we also, members of the (Federal Open Market Committee) make also judgmental kinds of sort of connections between what we see as the international environment and its impact on underlying growth momentum here as well as inflation as we try to think about how the broader global environment is going to impact the path of monetary policy and how that, in turn, will help with the attainment of our full employment and inflation goals.

So it's both kind of a forecasting set of tools that we have that can directly incorporate those, but it also affects different members differentially.

MR. MOSKOW: Yes, right there.

Q: Jordan Rosenfeld, Gelber Group.

If it looks like a majority of other FOMC members are open to a rate hike in coming months, would you dissent? (Laughter.)

MS. BRAINARD: (Laughs.) So the best I can do is to tell you a little bit about, you know, sort of my broader outlook. And I tried to go through that in some detail you know, how I'm thinking about the current underlying features of the U.S. economy and why they may be different than they were pre-crisis, and very specifically what are the sources of those differences, and then how would that condition my policy views. And you know, again, because the we've seen this persistent undershooting on inflation; the Phillips curve seems to be flatter inflation expectations seem to be well anchored on the upside but a little soft on the downside; because the labor market does potentially have still-untapped slack; because of the international environment that does have some risks and clearly disinflationary pressures, weak demand; and because of the low neutral rate, all of those things lead me to be somewhat asymmetric in my policy approach.

That said, policy, you know, is data-dependent. And my assessment, as well as those of other members, will continue to be based on the evolution of the data. And that's why, you know, I look forward to continuing to assess the data in the months ahead.

MR. MOSKOW: OK, we have a question all the way in the back. Someone had their hand up. Yes?

Q: Hi. Jason Lange with Reuters.

This morning Republican presidential candidate Donald Trump said that the Fed was keeping interest rates low bowing to political pressure. I wonder if you could comment on that allegation. I know it's not an area that the Fed likes to broach much, but Trump mentioned it in the morning.

MS. BRAINARD: So I'm not going to comment specifically on that.

But let me just step back a bit and just say that, you know, Congress institutionally created by design an institution in the Federal Reserve Board that, by design, is supposed to formulate monetary policy in a way that is very focused on two clearly specified objectives that are given to us in the statute: maximum employment and price stability. And so the institution is designed to ensure that independence from the executive branch. And the clarity of our objectives, which are given to us by statute, is absolutely the focus of the deliberations of the FOMC and the focus of our work in monetary policy.

MR. MOSKOW: We were talking about you've worked in the executive branch, so we were talking about at lunch the difference between working in the executive branch and working at the Fed. You might want to just mention your impressions. (Chuckles.)

MS. BRAINARD: Absolutely. They are institutionally very different. And the Federal Reserve Board in its culture, in its values, by design, and in its institution is independent in its making of monetary policy, and again, under very clear objectives given in statute by Congress.

MR. MOSKOW: OK. I had a question right up here in the front.

Q: Hi. My name's Doug Gray, a board member and Marshall Street Capital.

The question is the balance between fiscal policy and monetary policy. If there were a more aggressive fiscal policy in this country, what would the Federal Board's perspective be? I mean, I know you look at the data, but how do you strike a balance between and frankly, even motivate the rest of the executive branch and others to think about fiscal policy? It seems to me you're trying to sort of compensate for some weakness in some other places.

MS. BRAINARD: So, you know, I think there, too, while we're careful not to, you know, get into the specifics on the content of a fiscal program or it is clear and there was a lot of discussion about this at the recent conference among central bankers; it was international in nature that at this juncture, where aggregate demand seems to be weak globally and, you know, this has been a slow recovery here in the U.S. but, you know, even slower abroad, with bigger challenges abroad, that this is a moment where fiscal policy could really play a role in supporting both aggregate demand, but also in helping to really strengthen the productive potential here, and of course abroad there's potential for fiscal policy to do more as well.

MR. MOSKOW: Any other questions? Yes, in the back.

Q: Yes, hi. Shayndi Raice with The Wall Street Journal.

I just wanted to know, what would you need to see to be supportive of the removal of accommodation?

MS. BRAINARD: So, again, I think if you look at the recent developments, it's a mixed picture.

There's some welcome progress continuing on the labor market. That's come mainly from bringing people back into the labor force. The unemployment rate has largely moved sideways over the last year, where we have had continued improvements on labor-force participation, particularly among prime-age workers, as well as job growth of payrolls of 180,000, on average, per month. So that's been a positive. Personal consumption expenditure has been very strong, and at least with the latest data point moving into the third quarter we see continued strength there. And that augurs well, I think, for the third quarter. Consumer sentiment remains strong. And of course, financial conditions have eased since earlier in the year.

Against that, we have three quarters of just about 1% (annual rate of) growth, a big step down from the same period of last year. We've had very weak net exports, very weak business investment, weak corporate profitability and manufacturing.

And so, going forward, it would it would be important, I think, to see some of those spending indicators starting to really move in a more positive direction going into the third and fourth quarter.

And of course, the second leg of our dual mandate, inflation, has now been below (the Fed's 2%) target for 51 months. We've seen some slippage to the downside in inflation expectations. And so the recent movement in inflation, it's now been it's now at about 1.6% over the previous 12 months. That is an improvement since last year; still noticeably below 2%. And I'm going to be watching carefully, looking for signs of further progress there as well.

MR. MOSKOW: Yes, right here?

Q: My name's Carol McGillivray, and I actually had two questions.

I'm just curious, as someone who's obviously clearly I appreciate your layout of the what you see as the new normal and why. But how long does it take almost, statistically, before you know the new normal is the normal, for one question? (Laughter.)

And my other question is about the employment changes or the male population not participating. How much is that structural, with what people are talking about, about with the new-age jobs versus old manufacturing jobs? I mean, is that a big factor in what's happening here?

MS. BRAINARD: Yeah, so I think on the latter question the size of the population that's engaged in the, you know, sort of online gig type of jobs I think is tiny, less than one percentage point, although the increase there has been has been rapid. But it's still extremely small according to research by some notable labor economists.

On the first point, the four features and the fifth kind of policy conclusion that I draw, those are all things that seem to me to be proving somewhat persistent and seem like notable departures from pre-crisis. It's very difficult to say with precision how persistent they are likely to be.

Some of them are, you know, likely to be with us for a longer time than other. And, of course, these are things that are measured with some imprecision as well. And, of course, the natural rate of unemployment has a lot of imprecision around it. The neutral rate is something that is estimated also, you know, with a lot of imprecision around it.

So, you know, I think as monetary policy makers we do the best we can to construct a coherent framework for understanding the evolution of the data and what it tells us about the likely attainment of our goals. But we also have to be quite humble about the amount of, you know, imprecision. A lot of, you know, these are estimates that have probability distributions around them in many cases or very large confidence intervals.

MR. MOSKOW: Yes, right here in the front.

Q: Thank you. Governor Brainard, you spoke about how you think you have the tools, should there be inflation, should there be the need to address. Do you think there's been any evolution in the responsiveness of the economy to monetary measures, in particular the lags? How long you think it takes from let's say you were to see a burst of wage inflation and the Fed were then to act at its next meeting. How long would it take for that action to kick in?

MS. BRAINARD: So it's a really good question. I don't have a great answer for you on that, although it's something that obviously matters a great deal.

My sense is that the responsiveness through financial conditions is relatively rapid now. And we've really seen that again over the last since, you know, June of 2014 as expectations of divergence between the U.S. and foreign economies started to rise. We saw a very immediate response through the exchange rate. And so there does seem to be, at least through the financial transmission channel, a pretty rapid responsiveness.

You know, my sense is, again, I won't go back through the reasons that I gave earlier, but for a variety of reasons that if we were to see an unexpected strength and demand, given the state of the global economy, that we would be able to respond to that very effectively with a relatively moderate policy response relative to what might have been necessary pre-crisis.

MR. MOSKOW: Yes, in the back.

Q: Hello, Governor Brainard. I'm Karen Nye with D.S. Economics.

And I'm curious with respect to fiscal policy, if in the various scenarios that you on the FOMC and the staff make up in terms of what might happen with the economy, are you considering the 2016 elections, how it turns out? And I don't mean just the presidency, but the mix between the House and the Senate in terms of what fiscal policies might pass given different permutations after the election.

MS. BRAINARD: So there's lots of different sources of macroeconomic uncertainty. And, you know, we try to make the best kind of assessment of the economy as we can, recognizing that there are many sources of macro uncertainty out there.

But we don't, at least I don't, and I don't think generally there is any granular consideration given to anything in that arena. I think we try to be very focused on the macroeconomic objectives that we have. We look at the data as it's coming in against those objectives and how they pan out relative to our economic outlook.

MR. MOSKOW: OK, this will have to be the last question, one right here.

Q: Thank you. Michael Wood, Australian consul-general.

You've indicated you're more concerned about the capacity of the Fed to respond to negative surprises on the downside. And I wondered if you could share with us your views on the options available to the Fed in the event of a surprise on the downside.

I'm thinking personally of a resumption of quantitative easing or negative interest rates.

MS. BRAINARD: So as you probably may have gathered from my remarks, for me the real focus has to be on aligning our policies with achieving our objectives. And that really is the focus at the moment and, in particularly, making sure that we achieve the 2% inflation target, which is symmetric, which is not a ceiling, and maximum employment. And so this is really the focus.

As I said earlier, I think that the policy space using conventional tools is much greater on the upside than it is on the downside, is likely to be that for some time, and that other tools which have proven their efficacy here and abroad nonetheless are less well-understood, have greater uncertainties around them.

And so, again, my great, you know, sort of emphasis would be on trying to preserve and protect the progress that we've made here at home.

MR. MOSKOW: Well, Governor Brainard, on behalf of everyone here and on behalf of the council we want to just thank you for joining us today. This has been an excellent presentation and discussion.

MS. BRAINARD: Thank you. Thank you very much. (Applause.)

Pro Private Markets
Shawn Carolan Returns to Lead Consumer Team at Menlo Ventures

By Deborah Gage 
423 words
8 September 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Menlo Ventures Managing Director Shawn Carolan, who stepped away from the Silicon Valley venture firm to run Handle Inc., an email startup he founded, has returned to invest full-time.

Mr. Carolan is also building a team at Menlo Ventures that will invest in consumer startups, an area that the firm expects to grow as new technologies such as natural-language processing, machine learning and computer vision become more widely used.

The firm, which is based on Menlo Park's Sand Hill Road, plans to open an office in San Francisco and will hire an additional partner and two associates, according to Mr. Carolan.

Before he left for Handle, Mr. Carolan, who joined Menlo Ventures in 2002 and stayed full-time until 2014, invested in several prominent companies, including Siri, which became the foundation for Apple Inc.'s voice recognition system after Apple acquired the company in 2010.

He is a board observer at Uber Technologies Inc. and a board member at Roku Inc., the streaming video service.

"We think of Shawn as one of our best thinkers in new emerging consumer areas," which are now capable of creating global companies and winner-takes-all markets, said Menlo Managing Director Mark Siegel.

Also, he said, Mr. Carolan has the skills required to find the types of entrepreneurs who launch consumer startups. They tend to be younger and have new ideas, "and the way you find these entrepreneurs and find these ideas is quite different it is through networks of people," Mr. Siegel said.

Mr. Carolan said he returned to investing because he's better at looking across companies and finding patterns than he is at running companies. Handle is being restructured under a new CEO.

"I believe that technology is the thing uniquely suited to scale across the entire globe, and when you find an important problem and software or combination of hardware or software that can be out to a billion people, it means a lot to me to solve that," Mr. Carolan said.

Menlo Ventures raised two new funds recently a $400 million early stage fund in 2015 and a $250 million special opportunities fund in April for "early-growth" investments and currently makes about half of its investments in consumer companies, with the other half going to startups that sell software to businesses.

Although the firm won't increase the amount of money it invests in consumer companies, "there will certainly be an increased concentration," Mr. Siegel said.

Goldman Gives Away Its Winning Formula

By Justin Baer 
1,116 words
8 September 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Gary Cohn, Goldman Sachs Group Inc.'s president, once mused to colleagues it would take $5 billion before he'd be persuaded to share the firm's prized software that helped make it a trading powerhouse.

Now, Goldman is handing it out to clients for free.

Called Securities DataBase, or SecDB, the system is Goldman's prime tool for measuring risk and analyzing the prices of securities. It calculates 23 billion prices across 2.8 million positions daily and has played a crucial role in many of the seminal moments of the firm's recent history, including its controversial trading just ahead of the financial crisis.

Goldman had guarded it closely, resisting offers from rivals such as Deutsche Bank AG to license the database. One former partner recalls huddling with Mr. Cohn a decade ago to ponder what a licensing deal would be worth. Mr. Cohn told his colleagues he definitely wouldn't do it for $1 billion. For $5 billion? Maybe, he said, according to the former partner.

"It was such a competitive advantage," Mr. Cohn said recently.

Today, many of the edges that once made Goldman's traders feared and admired have been blunted. New rules have limited banks' trading risks, and made it costly to hold large inventories of stocks and bonds on their books. And electronic trading has squeezed margins.

Traders and executives tap into SecDB for information about how to price securities, and how the value of those assets may change with a twist on the dial on any one of thousands of potential variables. That information can be used to analyze potential trades -- and then to monitor the risks posed by those positions.

What made it the envy of Wall Street, though, was its ability to scale up to include new classes of securities, new trading desks, even whole businesses. And the data it harnessed was all in one place. Megamergers left rivals with a hodgepodge of different systems and different factions of employees loyal to each of them. Goldman avoided big acquisitions, evading issues that would slow its ability to track risks.

Thus, Goldman's new gambit: Deploy its technology to win more business from clients. Many of those tools are being offered in the form of web-based applications that customers can customize and operate on their own.

"It's the content, tools and analytics we've been working on for decades, and we're putting it in the hands of clients," said R. Martin Chavez, Goldman's chief information officer.

Some investors and clients harbor doubts that Goldman can pivot so sharply.

"I'm not sure their core competency is being a tech company," said Jason Brady, chief executive of Thornburg Investment Management, which manages $55 billion and trades with Goldman. "So it's a shift, and I'm not sure I want that to be their role."

The trick for Goldman will be to win over clients like Jeffrey Young, who co-heads an investing unit within Ramius LLC, a New York money manager. A Goldman saleswoman introduced Mr. Young to a Marquee app during a choppy period in the markets, when he needed regular updates on the bespoke derivatives he had bought from Goldman.

"They're trying to be more proactive -- instead of just saying 'we need to satisfy what the client wants,' they're saying 'let's make sure they're as happy as possible,'" he said. "They're more open with a lot of their intellectual property."

Goldman is seeking its niche in a crowded field. BlackRock Inc. offers its own risk-management system, called Aladdin, and Bloomberg LP's terminals remain a ubiquitous presence on trading floors.

Mr. Chavez is an alumnus of J. Aron, the commodities-trading arm where many of Goldman's leaders, including Chairman and Chief Executive Lloyd Blankfein and his top deputy, Mr. Cohn, got their start.

SecDB's run within Goldman began when Marc Spilker, a currency-options trader, sought a pricing database with the flexibility to add new derivatives. He turned to Armen Avanessians, a former Bell Labs engineer Mr. Blankfein had hired and the man one Goldman alum referred to, somewhat affectionately, as the "Darth Vader" of the strategists, or strats -- a team of engineers J. Aron had begun to assemble to work on computer models.

Mr. Avanessians handed the task to Michael Dubno, a college dropout and former videogame developer who had come to Goldman to work for famed economist Fischer Black. Two other engineers, Glenn Gribble and Kevin Lundeen, soon followed. Mr. Avanessians hired Mr. Chavez in 1993 to extend SecDB to J. Aron's commodities desk.

The SecDB system received an early test in 1998, when hedge fund Long-Term Capital Management nearly collapsed. Goldman used the database to help analyze its exposure, an episode that helped raise SecDB's profile within the firm, according to people familiar with the matter.

Over time, SecDB spread to other parts of J. Aron and, ultimately, the entire firm. As Mr. Blankfein rose up the ranks, he insisted that SecDB and the strategists join him.

By the time the housing market boomed, SecDB had reached Goldman's firmwide risk and finance departments. "Thank goodness we did that when we did," recalled Robert Berry, who heads Goldman's market-risk team.

As the prices on securities tied to subprime mortgages began to decline in late 2006, Mr. Berry and other executives plotted out how the firm's positions performed against its models. They could soon tell something wasn't right.

Goldman made some profitable bets against the housing market and, in some instances, began reducing exposure to other counterparties, even as many of their peers and clients remained bullish. Later, the bank's moves were roundly criticized as evidence it put its own interests above those of its customers. Goldman has defended its actions.

Mr. Chavez left Goldman in the late 1990s, eventually founding a Silicon Valley startup he sold in 2004. While running that company, Mr. Chavez filed an application for a patent on a system some Goldman partners found similar to SecDB. They were incensed that Mr. Chavez had failed to credit Goldman, and brought it to Mr. Cohn's attention, people familiar with the matter said.

Goldman sent Mr. Chavez "a nasty letter," one person said. Mr. Cohn, though, declined to authorize a lawsuit. When Mr. Chavez sold his startup, Mr. Cohn persuaded him to rejoin Goldman.

Goldman elevated Mr. Chavez to CIO in 2013. The flagship in his strategy is Marquee, a series of applications that allows clients to tap into SecDB and other systems.

TPG gains control of McAfee in $4.2bn deal

Hannah Kuchler in San Francisco 
546 words
8 September 2016
00:07
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

TPG is buying a majority stake in Intel Security, returning the antivirus company once again to a standalone software maker called McAfeebut at a valuation of roughly half what the chipmaker bought it for.

The new McAfee will be spun out of Intel at a valuation of $4.2bn, down 45 per cent fromthe $7.7bn Intel paid in 2010, after six years in which it has received profits from McAfee but failed to deliver on its promise of embedding the group s cyber security capabilities in Intel s chips.

TPG, the private equity firm, will take a 51 per cent stake and Intel will retain the remaining 49 per cent. Intel will receive $3.1bn of cash, $2bn of which will come from new debt taken on by McAfee, and the rest of which will come from TPG s $1.1bn equity investment.

Jim Coulter, co-founder and co-chief executive of TPG, said McAfee could sharpen its focus and respond faster to the  rapidly-evolving  cyber security sector as an independent company. TPG had been in discussions with Intel for 10 months, seeing off competition from other private equity bidders in the early stages.

Private equity buyers have been hunting for cyber security companies, attracted by strong revenue prospects from a sector whose clients remain concerned about the potential damage caused by cyber attacks. So far this year, Vista Equity Partners has bought Ping Identity for an undisclosed sum, while Bain Capital sold Bluecoat to Symantec for almost double what it paid for the company the year before.

TPG has invested in cyber security companies ZScaler, which secures the cloud, and Tanium, which helps companies deploy security fixes rapidly.

McAfee will be one of the largest standalone security companies in a very fragmented industry, facing competition from a new generation of start-ups backed by a flood of venture capital pouring into the sector. It will be led by Chris Young, who managed Intel Security when it was part of Intel, and is likely to use its new independence to acquire cyber security start-ups to bring newer technologies to its established platforms.

In a letter to employees and customers, Mr Young said McAfee would emerge from the deal in a position of  greater strength , working towards its  noble cause  of protecting consumers, corporations and governments from cyber criminals. In the first half of this year, revenue at Intel Security increased 11 per cent to $1.1bn, while operating income rose almost fourfold to $182m.

Intel in 2010 hoped to integrate McAfee s security products on to its chips, promising the ability to detect threats at a deeper level. But the company has failed to complete this plan after six years and many of the senior leadership from before the deal have departed.

Intel is now trying to position itself as a leading chipmaker for the  internet of things  era, acquiring computer vision and deep learning start-ups. Brian Krzanich, its chief executive, said the chipmaker would continue to work closely with the new standalone McAfee and prioritise security.

The Financial Times first reported that Intel was weighing up the sale of its cyber security business in June.

Markets
Global Bank Regulators Delay New Capital Rules; Little-noticed shift in the timetable came amid intense resistance from European policy makers

By Donna Borak 
524 words
8 September 2016
00:41
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON International regulators have pushed back their year-end deadline to finalize controversial new bank capital rules, amid intense resistance from European policy makers.

The little-noticed shift in the timetable came "http://www.fsb.org/wp-content/uploads/FSB-Chairï¿½ï¿½ï¿½s-letter-to-G20-Leaders-in-advance-of-their-meeting-in-Hangzhou-on-4-5-September..pdf"issued by Mark Carney , who chairs the Financial Stability Board , a committee created in the wake of the financial crisis to bolster the global financial safety net. Mr. Carney said that long-awaited rules affecting the balance sheets of the world's largest banks would be completed "by early 2017." That was in contrast to statements from other officials throughout the year pledging action by the end of 2016.

Mr. Carney, who is also governor of the Bank of England, was referring to new guidelines being crafted by a separate global body, the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision , which has spent the year attempting to address weaknesses spotlighted by the financial crisis. Officials are considering new rules that would limit how banks calculate their own risks and require them to hew more closely to formulas created by regulators.

Basel officials have said the proposals are aimed at creating more consistency among banks, and aren't designed to force them to hold more expensive capital on their books.

But European officials have objected to the effort, raising concerns that the changes could significantly increase bank capital requirements, depressing bank profitability and undermining the continent's already sluggish economy.

That is a stark contrast with the U.S., where regulators led by the Federal Reserve are openly pushing for still-higher capital on the books of the big banks.

In his Aug. 30 letter, Mr. Carney reiterated a commitment that upcoming changes to the so-called Basel III framework would "not significantly" increase overall capital requirements across the banking industry. Mr. Carney's statement was addressed to the leaders of the Group of 20 advanced and developing economies ahead of their two-day summit in China.

"http://www.g20.org/English/Dynamic/201609/t20160906_3396.html", the G-20 leaders said they were still pushing for completion of the Basel rules this year, dubbed the "Basel III" round, following two earlier iterations of such guidelines.

"We reiterate our support for the work by the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision to finalize the Basel III framework by the end of 2016, without further significantly increasing overall capital requirements across the banking sector, while promoting a level playing field," the statement said.

Karen Shaw Petrou, a managing partner of the policy analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc . said the Carney and G-20 statements appeared to be setting somewhat different targets to allow the parties to try to reconcile their differences.

Given the continuing opposition from some countries, she said, there is a new push by global regulators "to reach a tentative agreement by year-end, but not finalize a deal until there is another survey" to rebut the critics and "prove" that revised changes won't significantly increase capital requirements.

International Property: The U.K.: New Normal for U.K. Deals --- Wells Fargo's 'retrade' is one of the discount transactions following vote to exit the EU

By Art Patnaude 
851 words
7 September 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

LONDON -- In the tumultuous weeks following Britain's vote to leave the European Union, Wells Fargo & Co. restarted talks on a deal to buy an office-building development. So Wells sought to do what several other big property purchasers have done since the historic Brexit vote: negotiate a "retrade."

On July 18, Wells said it would spend nearly GBP 300 million ($399.1 million) to buy the property, slightly below what was in the works before the vote, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Wells Fargo will be using the building to house its London operations, which consist primarily of 850 employees who serve the bank's corporate, commercial and financial institution customers as they do business in Europe, the Middle East and Africa.

"We're making a long-term commitment here," said Frank Pizzo, regional president for Europe, Mideast and Africa region at Wells Fargo. Brexit didn't have "any impact on that position."

The deal happened at a time when the U.K. real-estate market was in chaos, with landlords' stocks tumbling and redemptions halted on some property funds. London office values have suffered the most thus far, according to experts, but some analysts have predicted a broad swath of U.K. commercial real-estate prices could fall further over the next 12 months.

Retrades have made up a large portion of the transactions that have taken place since the June 23 vote, according to market participants.

But the discounts, as reflected by the Wells Fargo deal, have been less than those suffered by sellers in the U.K. and other real-estate markets in previous shocks, most recently the 2008 global financial collapse. Back then markets essentially ground to a halt, said Anne Breen, head of real-estate research and strategy at asset manager Standard Life Investments, one of the fund managers that has halted redemptions.

"Today in the U.K., the backdrop is quite different. There remains liquidity," Ms. Breen said. "The banks are still open for business. Investors are still looking at property yield and think it's attractive. The REITs [real-estate investment trusts] are very well financed and structured relative to previous cycles."

Part of the reason the market has remained relatively buoyant: currency fluctuations.

Wells Fargo, for example, benefited from the steep drop in the sterling compared with dollars following the referendum, which essentially knocked another 12% off the sticker price.

Still, in the weeks following the vote, market messages have been mixed. The good news has been that shares of many listed property companies have rebounded, with the notable exception being owners of London office and residential property.

On the other hand, managers of about half the open ended funds that focused on U.K. property have continued to freeze redemptions. Some of those that have kept their redemption window open have imposed extra costs on investors who have wanted out.

Such bargains are prompting some investors to sniff for buying opportunities. Norwegian sovereign-wealth fund manager Norges Bank Investment Management purchased a high profile Oxford Street commercial building in mid-July for GBP 124 million from Aberdeen Asset Management PLC, one of the asset managers that has continued making redemptions.

Values of some commercial property in London had moved into record territory in recent years after recovering from the losses they suffered following the 2008 market crash.

But even before the Brexit vote, signs were mounting that values were beginning to decline. The value of U.K. commercial real estate declined 0.3% in June, according to an index compiled by MSCI Inc., a research firm.

The surprise vote raised questions about when, and how, the U.K. will break off its richly entwined relationship with the EU.

The terms of the exit will have an impact on the U.K. economy and influence how U.K.-based businesses are allowed to operate within the EU, both major factors for property investors. The MSCI index fell 2.8% in July, the biggest decline since March 2009.

Deal activity also slowed. About $5 billion of property transactions have been completed since the vote, less than half of the $12.9 billion of deals during the same period last year and the GBP 13.4 billion in 2014, according to property data-firm Real Capital Analytics.

Other buyers also are retrading deals. German property investor Deka Immobilien GmbH bought a 14-story office building in Manchester in August for GBP 164 million, less than the price that had been settled on before the referendum, according to a statement from the firm.

But so far, highly distressed discounts have been outliers. And because property values haven't immediately collapsed, potential sellers might offer up to 5% off pre-Brexit values, but are typically unwilling to negotiate any lower, said Bruce Dear, head of London real estate at U.K. law firm Eversheds.

FRONT PAGE - COMPANIES & MARKETS
Investment banks suffer 15% decline in revenues as M&A drop hurts margins

By Ben McLannahan in New York 
366 words
6 September 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The world's biggest investment banks saw combined revenues sink 15 per cent in the first half of this year, the most since the aftermath of the financial crisis, underlining the urgency of taking radical measures to boost returns to shareholders.

The second quarter saw more activity than the first, where there were simultaneous falls across stocks, bonds and commodities and a dearth of companies doing deals. But the rebound was not strong enough to avert a fall in total revenues from investment banking for the top 12 banks, according to figures from Coalition, a London-based group that combines public information with independent research and validation by a network of market participants.

The banks' revenues from trading stocks and bonds, and advising companies on mergers and capital raising, came to $79bn in the first half, down from $93.3bn in 2015. That marked the steepest year-on-year fall since a 25 per cent drop between the first halves of 2009 and 2010. The peak was $140bn in the first half of 2009.

George Kuznetsov, head of research and analytics at Coalition, said the prospects for a better second half of 2016 looked good, as the third quarter had continued in a similar vein as the second. Even so, he said, no bank expected its own revenues to rise more than 5 per cent or 6 per cent next year, as tougher rules on capital and risk-taking continued to weigh heavily on the industry.

"Investment banks are realising there is no top-line relief, capital allocation from [parent companies] probably won't change much, and regulations won't change much. So improvement in returns becomes dependent on optimising the cost base or grabbing market share," Mr Kuznetsov said.

Banks have tried to keep a lid on expenses by relocating support staff to cheaper locations and by tying pay - their biggest cost - more closely to income. At Goldman Sachs, for example, compensation and benefits dropped 13 per cent in the second quarter from the previous year, exactly in line with net revenues.

The world's top 10 investment banks have already paid out almost as much in fines and penalties so far this year as they did in the 12 months of 2015, dashing hopes that the outlay for misdeeds had begun to taper off.

They paid $9.8bn for the first eight months of 2016, meaning that this year's total will almost certainly top the $10.4bn for 2015, according to data compiled by analytics firm Corlytics.

The sums are a far cry from the height of the fining boom , when annual penalties for the 10 banks reached $58.2bn in 2014 and $29.8bn for 2013, as they paid up for everything from mis-selling mortgage bonds to rigging foreign exchange rates.

But investors are worried about the outlook. "The main litigation issues are solved at least for most of the banks and therefore there's an assumption in the market that further down the road we don't see any huge litigation," said Ingo Speich, portfolio manager at Frankfurt-based Union Investment. "The risk is that at some point something could pop up that affects the entire banking sector that nobody has on their radar screen."

The 10 banks - the five biggest Wall Street operations plus Deutsche Bank, Credit Suisse, UBS, HSBC and Barclays - are also eyeing the future cautiously. They began 2016 with $43.7bn of collective provisions to pay future fines and litigation, up from the $36.3bn they had at the start of 2015. Outstanding issues include US mortgage mis-selling fines for European banks and Russian trading issues for Deutsche .

Goldman Sachs topped the penalty list for 2016 so far, paying out more than $5bn over mis-selling mortgage-backed securities. Several European banks have yet to settle similar cases and those fines are expected over the coming months.

Philippe Morel, global head of capital markets at Boston Consulting, said that while banks had invested massively to avoid past problems, new regulation could spark a fresh enforcement round.

Laurent Frings of Aberdeen Asset Management said some of the big issues from the financial crisis were winding down but provisioning for smaller fines had become "part and parcel" of what investors factor in when valuing banks.

COMMENT
Echoes of 2008 as danger signs are ignored

By Gillian Tett on Finance 
829 words
2 September 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
09
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Ten years ago when the world's central bankers gathered in Jackson Hole for their annual retreat in the majestic Teton Range in Wyoming, their community was marked by an intellectual divide. This was not between "left-" and "right-" wing economists; instead, the crucial faultline was whether the mandarins did or did not care about finance.

Back then, while many participants happily debated in the highlands of macroeconomic discourse, some central bank officials - such as William White, chief economist at the Bank for International Settlements, or his colleague Claudio Borio - were deeply worried about what was happening down in the the weeds of global finance. In particular they feared excesses were building with newfangled instruments that many traded but few truly understood, such as credit default swaps. However, those Cassandras were a minority; most central bankers were so obsessed with seemingly benign "real" economy issues such as inflation, unemployment or growth rates.

A decade later this intellectual split has closed - a little bit. For after the financial crisis exploded in 2007, it become painfully clear to policymakers that finance sometimes can hurt the "real" economy. What was happening in those seemingly arcane derivatives markets in 2006, for example, was fuelling a mortgage and corporate borrowing binge - and when that imploded it tipped the wider economy into recession. So after 2008, central bankers implemented reforms that are supposed to force them to try to understand how the finer details of financial markets might end up damaging the global economy again.

In the US, the Federal Reserve has created a Financial Stability Oversight Council , which is supposed to take a joined-up view of finance. An Office for Financial Research has been established to use Big Data techniques to track how money moves around the world with far greater accuracy than before. Meanwhile in the UK, the Bank of England has established a Financial Policy Committee to track the financial system; this complements its interest rate-setting Monetary Policy Committee, which tends to watch what is happening in the real economy. And in the academic sphere, the study of finance is no longer relegated to business schools; it is creeping into high-status university economics departments too.

But this revolution was never wholesale - and may now be going into retreat. Consider, for example, Jackson Hole. This year's gathering featured some debate about "real economy" issues. There was also extensive discussion about whether central banks need new tools to boost growth and fight deflation.

But what was notably absent from the agenda was a full-blown discussion about the issue that is on the minds of most financial practitioners: namely some of the peculiar distortions that are developing in the weeds of modern finance. Anybody who deals with financial markets on a micro level today - as a banker, asset manager or corporate treasurer - knows that many asset prices are extraordinarily elevated.

Never mind the most obvious headline-grabbing points, such as the record-high levels of US equities or the fact that more than $13tn worth of global government and corporate bonds carry negative rates. What is equally striking are the numerous tiny signals that tend to go ignored: the fact that in Denmark mortgages now carry negative interest rates; or that in Japan, the central bank has gobbled up so many equities that it owns about 15 per cent of the free float of companies such as Fast Retailing, and is committed to buying 6tn worth more exchange traded funds this year.

These peculiarities are creating headaches for pension funds, insurance companies and banks, to name but a few. But the Jackson Hole tribe barely mentioned these at all. The only voice in the central bank community that has been shouting loudly about these numbers is the BIS; its latest annual report issues a chilling indictment about the unsustainable nature of these peculiarly low rates and the dangers of "debt-fuelled growth". But, this has largely been ignored - as in 2006.

This is ominous. In the short term, those sky-high asset prices seem unlikely to produce a full-blown 2008-style crisis; there is less leverage in the western financial system today than in 2006 and banking is more robust because of post-crisis reforms. Moreover, it seems that those rock-bottom low rates reflect long-term structural issues - such as ageing demographics and falling productivity - as much as central bank interventions. This suggests they could endure for a long time.

But just because policymakers have become inured to a deeply peculiar financial world, that does not mean that it is "normal" or healthy; or not any more than it was in 2006. We had all better hope that by the summer of 2017 a debate about finance gets a proper billing at Jackson Hole. And that the BIS keeps shouting loudly about this distorted system - and is finally heard.

gillian.tett@ft.com

Swift, Citing Fresh Attacks, Talks Tough on Security

Global money-transfer network Swift is in a bind: Regulators are "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-regulators-step-up-scrutiny-around-swift-1472060171" to the Brussels-based cooperative amid a wave of cyberattacks on its users, at the same time as some banks have apparently been slow to improve the security of their links to Swift.

The problem: Customers are responsible for their own security. But Swift, formally called the Society for Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunication, says it has limited power to make users comply with system guidelines on its own.

The company can shut banks out of its network, but has rarely done so. Chief Executive Gottfried Leibbrandt"http://www.wsj.com/articles/swift-ceo-surprised-by-extent-of-cyberattacks-1464909418" that this could lead to firms using newer, untested channels.

Swift rolled out a plan for beefing up security earlier this year, in the wake of cyberattacks on customers in Bangladesh, Vietnam and Ecuador. But it didn't include details of how the new standards would be enforced. "We can come out with guidelines for air gaps, then an interesting question is,  Who will enforce this, and Swift, why don't you enforce it?' " said Mr. Leibbrandt. "As far as I am concerned, nothing is off the table on this."

Swift has been working on detecting patterns of user behavior to spot anomalies, and a framework for audits to be conducted by independent third parties. The network connects more than 11,000 institutions across more than 200 countries and territories. But some users have been reluctant to inform Swift of cyber incidents, leaving the company continually urging them to report problems. Meanwhile, others have been slow to adopt new security measures.

On Tuesday, Swift took a fresh tack, telling customers it would alert regulators and trading partners if banks didn't take the specific step of installing new software updates by a Nov. 19 deadline.

In a letter sent to customers Tuesday viewed by The Wall Street Journal, Swift said it reserves "the right to share information on non-compliant customers with regulators, authorities and/or their counterparties."

Also in the letter, Swift disclosed new attacks at customer sites, warning the new software protections were needed because cyberattacks using stolen Swift credentials were continuing. Swift said "a good number" of the fresh attacks had been thwarted, however.

A spokeswoman for Swift declined to comment on the nature, number and location of the firms newly targeted. In the letter, the company said it had no indication its core messaging services had been compromised.

The communication shows Swift is under pressure to tighten up security. One of its former chief executives "http://www.wsj.com/articles/swift-banking-network-struggles-with-wave-of-cyberattacks-1463786328" the company hadn't been aggressive enough on security at user sites.

Swift said it last introduced new software in July. The latest update includes a two-stage process to authenticate payment instructions; these commonly include a password plus a separately generated code. Many large banks have two-factor authentication systems of their own, but Swift software now comes with a version built in.

In the letter, Swift also reminded customers that they are required to report problems to the network operator, and that their first line of cyberdefense should be turning off certain message types or recipients in their Swift systems.

In July, Swift "http://www.wsj.com/articles/swift-hires-cybersecurity-firms-following-customer-breaches-1468240947" at BAE Systems PLC, a London-based defense firm whose researchers have been blogging about cyber incidents at Swift user sites, and Fox-IT, based in the Netherlands, to help in its response to the attacks.

International Experiences With Macroprudential Policies

While macroprudential policies developed in the wake of the financial crisis have not yet been through an entire financial cycle, global regulators believe that many aspects of their postcrisis experiences are useful for future policy making. "http://www.bis.org/publ/othp26.htm" by the Bank for International Settlements, the Financial Stability Board, and the International Monetary Fund released ahead of next week's G-20 Leaders' Summit in Hangzhou, China spells out a number of those macroprudential elements. It calls for clear regulatory mandates, transparency and accountability mechanisms, and a "broad range of policy tools to address systemic risk over time and from across the financial system."

Steps Policy Makers Can Take If a New Financial Crisis Hits

When the next crisis hits, policy makers will again face the age-old question, "https://www.brookings.edu/opinions/what-will-government-do-when-the-next-financial-crisis-hits/": Should they take politically unpopular steps to mitigate an even greater problem? Dodd-Frank curtailed the Federal Reserve's ability to use its emergency lending authority to aid a single firm and required the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. to seek congressional approval to use its systemic risk exception. Mr. Klein writes those legal changes will make it tougher for regulators to take radical, and unpopular, action. But a revamped orderly liquidation authority removes some of the need for these other tools to be used, he says.

The Property Report: In Airbnb Era, a Refi Conundrum

By Peter Rudegeair 
1,052 words
31 August 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B10
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Room-rental services such as Airbnb Inc. are blurring the line between residential and commercial property. That is causing problems for some homeowners looking to refinance mortgages.

Big banks including Bank of America Corp. and Wells Fargo & Co. are subjecting some refinance customers who rent rooms to additional scrutiny. Some borrowers have been told they were no longer eligible for certain kinds of loans or would have to pay higher interest rates, according to the customers.

"This is kind of novel," said Jeffrey Naimon, a consumer-finance attorney and partner at law firm BuckleySandler LLP. "I don't think the market has gotten its arms around it."

The issue for lenders is how to classify loans in the Airbnb age. Historically, that has been easy: A house usually was either a principal residence or an investment property. Mortgages on the latter are often viewed as riskier because owners had less of a personal connection to the house and rental income isn't always reliable.

Now, the distinction isn't so clear-cut. Online-rental services are spreading rapidly; Airbnb's website had 455,223 active listings in the U.S. as of July, up 80% from a year earlier, according to research firm YipitData. That is posing challenges to lenders and frustrating some customers, illustrating how fast-paced technological change can reverberate in unexpected ways.

The issue comes up when borrowers report income from services such as Airbnb when applying for a new loan, often in hope of improving their credit profile. That, they hope, can lead to a better interest rate on a loan.

Brad Severtson, a resident of Seattle's Ballard neighborhood, earned roughly $30,000 last year from renting out a cottage in his Victorian home's backyard. He thought that would work in his favor when he applied in early 2016 to refinance a home-equity line of credit at Bank of America.

The bank turned him down, saying it didn't allow home-equity lines of credit on properties in which the homeowner is operating a business, including Airbnb. Mr. Severtson, a 61-year-old data scientist and former Rhodes scholar, was taken aback.

"Here's a bank I've had a relationship with for 30 years," he said. "The assumption to me was the more your income is, the less risk to them. That assumption was wrong."

Mr. Severtson ultimately refinanced with Umpqua Holdings Corp., a Portland, Ore.-based bank.

A Bank of America spokesman said the bank doesn't provide home-equity lines of credit on investment properties.

He said the bank would consider a customer's primary residence an investment property if there was a "material amount of commercial activity," but that "incremental renting" wouldn't be an issue.

A spokesman for Airbnb said such incidents "are incredibly rare."

Mortgage lenders typically apply tougher underwriting standards, including larger down payments and higher rates, to second homes or investment properties that owners don't live in for most of the year.

For mortgages made this past April that went toward the purchase of a home, lenders on average were willing to finance 84% of a property's value if the borrower intended to live there, according to data from real-estate analytics firm CoreLogic Inc. The average interest rate was 3.76%. For investment properties, lenders only financed 72% of the home's value, on average, and charged an average interest rate of 4.29%.

That is because borrowers have shown a greater propensity to default on investment-property loans. Cumulative losses on investment-property loans included in private-label mortgage bonds issued in the four years before the financial crisis reached nearly 20% in early 2016, according to data from Moody's Investors Service and ABSNet Loan. For owner-occupied mortgages, the loss rate was about 14%.

An additional worry: Defaults can trigger requests from mortgage investors and government agencies for a lender to repurchase the loan. So, many lenders proceed with caution when a loan doesn't fit neatly into a predefined category, although that is less true for "jumbo" mortgages on high-price homes that often stay on bank balance sheets.

For mainstream lenders in the postcrisis mortgage world, "the last thing they want is someone coming back to them later" with a request to repurchase soured loans, said Christopher Mayer, a professor of real estate at Columbia Business School.

That, though, can cause issues for some borrowers. Stephen Labovsky this spring applied to Wells Fargo to refinance the mortgage on his home in San Francisco's Glen Park neighborhood.

Mr. Labovsky, 72, and his wife were active Airbnb hosts and had registered their property with the city government. That permits them to rent space for an unlimited number of nights a year while they reside in the home and for no more than 90 nights when they aren't on the premises.

But because the couple were operating short-term rentals for much of the year, Mr. Labovsky said, Wells Fargo recommended he apply for a mortgage as if his home were an investment property. That would have increased the interest rate by up to 0.5 percentage point, he said. Mr. Labovsky, a retired filmmaker, stuck with his existing mortgage.

Greg Gwizdz, national sales manager for Wells Fargo's mortgage division, said the bank has no policy of restricting short-term rentals on its borrowers' properties.

He said that services like Airbnb haven't caused changes in the bank's position on what constitutes a primary residence or an investment property.

Still, he acknowledges there may be confusion within the industry. Similar questions were raised a few years ago about whether homeowners' traditional insurance policies would also apply to their Airbnb activity. The startup eventually responded by offering hosts free, primary liability coverage up to $1 million per incident.

In the case of mortgage refinancing, though, banks will likely have to gain more experience with Airbnb hosts.

"Some of the programs that are new that allow people to rent out their properties short term or in different ways that may not have existed 10 years ago may not be fully understood by every lender across America," Mr. Gwizdz said.
